You are the generator in an offline, import-grade GCSE standard-deck compiler.

Create exactly 4 high-value recall cards for EVERY supplied selectable curriculum topic (28 cards total). Use only the official source excerpts below. The topic is a course-scope container: a card may use a narrower descendant concept, but topicComponentId must remain the supplied selectable topic id and curriculumComponentId must be the exact supplied descendant id whose excerpt supports the answer.

Hard rules:
- Output JSON only, matching the schema.
- This is AQA GCSE Physics; do not import facts from another board or version.
- Every answer must be entailed by one source excerpt. sourceExcerpt must be ONE short passage of at most 400 characters and a character-for-character CONTIGUOUS substring copied from the selected component's exact text value. Prefer one sentence, clause or bullet. Preserve every space, line break, punctuation mark and character at that location. Never copy equation/diagram layout, reflow a line, insert a line break, join paragraphs or non-contiguous fragments, omit words or clauses from the middle, use ellipses, or emit control bytes. Before returning JSON, verify mentally that source.text.includes(sourceExcerpt) would be true. Give that component id and its page locator.
- Preserve source qualifiers exactly: do not turn "may", "typical", "without knowledge of", a listed sequence, or a limited example into a stronger universal, credential rule, or spatial convention.
- Exclude Higher-only content whenever ANY target offering is Foundation. Prefer shared core knowledge, never silently map Higher-only content to Foundation.
- Fronts must support unaided recall and be self-contained: never use unresolved phrases such as "these issues". Backs are concise canonical answers. Write a separate explanation. memoryTip may be null and should be null when no honest, non-contrived retrieval route exists; never restate the answer or force a gimmick. Every factual contrast or clause in the explanation must be directly supported by the cited excerpt; omit even generally true embellishment when the excerpt does not state it.
- Use one meaningful emoji from the supplied allowlist. It must orient without revealing the answer or a defining property of the answer. Do not use a generic book/note cue merely to mean "study". For security, encryption, lock, key or access questions, do not use 🔐.
- Supply exactly three or four unique choices. Exactly one isCorrect=true and its text equals back exactly. Use four total choices only when there are three distinct, plausible neighbouring misconceptions; use three total choices when a third distractor would be contrived or repetitive. Every choice has concise teaching feedback and every wrong choice has a specific misconception label. Do not make distractors by combining unrelated nonsense categories.
- Prefer durable definitions, relationships, sequence, comparison, methods and exact governing facts over trivia. Vary kinds within each topic.
- A card must retrieve a usable subject concept or method. Do not turn an assessment objective, curriculum heading, list of command verbs, or paired near-synonyms into content merely because it appears in the specification.
- IDs, concept keys and all choice keys are lowercase kebab-case and globally specific. Use stable choice keys a, b, c and d in displayed order. Do not mention answer controls in the front.
- Do not create quotation or plot cards from curriculum specifications.

Allowed kinds: definition, formula, process, test-result, unit, practical, fact, comparison, plot, quotation, character, theme, context, method, case-study, chronology, cause-consequence, interpretation, technique, structure.

Details of all skills are\ngiven in Key opportunities for skills development (page 184).\n124 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\n6.1.1.4 Power\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nPower is defined as the rate at which energy is transferred or the\nrate at which work is done.\npower = energy trans f erred\ntime\nP =\nE\nt\npower = work done\ntime\nP =\nW\nt\npower, P, in watts, W\nenergy transferred, E, in joules, J\ntime, t, in seconds, s\nwork done, W, in joules, J\nAn energy transfer of 1 joule per second is equal to a power of 1\nwatt.\nStudents should be able to give examples that illustrate the\ndefinition of power eg comparing two electric motors that both lift\nthe same weight through the same height but one does it faster\nthan the other.\nMS 3b, c\nStudents should be able to\nrecall and apply both\nequations.\n6.1.2 Conservation and dissipation of energy\n6.1.2.1 Energy transfers in a system\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nEnergy can be transferred usefully, stored or dissipated, but cannot\nbe created or destroyed.\nStudents should be able to describe with examples where there are\nenergy transfers in a closed system, that there is no net change to\nthe total energy.\nStudents should be able to describe, with examples, how in all\nsystem changes energy is dissipated, so that it is stored in less\nuseful ways. This energy is often described as being ‘wasted’.\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThe main energy resources available for use on Earth include: fossil\nfuels (coal, oil and gas), nuclear fuel, bio-fuel, wind, hydro-\nelectricity, electricity\ngeneration and heating.\nStudents should be able to:\n• describe the main energy sources available\n• distinguish between energy resources that are renewable and\nenergy resources that WS 3.5\nDescriptions of how energy resources are used to generate\nelectricity are not required.\nStudents should be able to:\n• consider the environmental issues that may arise from the use\nof different energy resources\n• show that science has the ability to identify environmental\nissues arising from the use of energy resources but not\nalways the power to deal with the issues because of political,\nsocial, ethical or economic considerations.\nWS 1.3, 1.4, 4.4\nMS 1c, 2c, 4a\n6.2 Electricity\nElectric charge is a fundamental property of matter everywhere. Understanding the difference in\nthe microstructure of conductors, semiconductors and insulators makes it possible to design\ncomponents and build electric circuits. Many circuits are powered with mains electricity, but\nportable electrical devices must use batteries of some kind.\nElectrical power fills the modern world with artificial light and sound, information and entertainment,\nremote sensing and control. The fundamentals of electromagnetism were worked out by scientists\nof the 19th century. However, power stations, like all machines, have a limited lifetime. WS 3.5\nDescriptions of how energy resources are used to generate\nelectricity are not required.\nStudents should be able to:\n• consider the environmental issues that may arise from the use\nof different energy resources\n• show that science has the ability to identify environmental\nissues arising from the use of energy resources but not\nalways the power to deal with the issues because of political,\nsocial, ethical or economic considerations.\nWS 1.3, 1.4, 4.4\nMS 1c, 2c, 4a\n6.2 Electricity\nElectric charge is a fundamental property of matter everywhere. Understanding the difference in\nthe microstructure of conductors, semiconductors and insulators makes it possible to design\ncomponents and build electric circuits. Many circuits are powered with mains electricity, but\nportable electrical devices must use batteries of some kind.\nElectrical power fills the modern world with artificial light and sound, information and entertainment,\nremote sensing and control. The fundamentals of electromagnetism were worked out by scientists\nof the 19th century. However, power stations, like all machines, have a limited lifetime. Many circuits are powered with mains electricity, but\nportable electrical devices must use batteries of some kind.\nElectrical power fills the modern world with artificial light and sound, information and entertainment,\nremote sensing and control. If we all\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 127\ncontinue to demand more electricity this means building new power stations in every generation –\nbut what mix of power stations can promise a sustainable future?\n6.2.1 Current, potential difference and resistance\n6.2.1.1 Standard circuit diagram symbols\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nCircuit diagrams use standard symbols.\nStudents should be able to draw and interpret circuit diagrams.\nWS 1.2\n6.2.1.2 Electrical charge and current\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nFor electrical charge to flow through a closed circuit the circuit must\ninclude a source of potential difference.\n128 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nElectric current is a flow of electrical charge. The size of the electric\ncurrent is the rate of flow of electrical charge. Charge flow, current\nand time are linked by the equation:\ncharge f low = current × time\nQ = I t\ncharge flow, Q, in coulombs, C\ncurrent, I, in amperes, A (amp is acceptable for ampere)\ntime, t, in seconds, s\nA current has the same value at any point in a single closed loop.\nMS 3b, c\nStudents should be able to\nrecall and apply this\nequation.\n6.2.1.3 Current, resistance and potential difference\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThe current (I) through a component depends on both the\nresistance (R) of the component and the potential difference (V)\nacross the component. The greater the resistance of the component\nthe smaller the current for a given potential difference (pd) across\nthe component.\nQuestions will be set using the term potential difference. Students\nwill gain credit for the correct use of either potential difference or\nvoltage.\nCurrent, potential difference or resistance can be calculated using\nthe equation:\npotential di f f erence = current × resistance\nV = I R\npotential difference, V, in volts, V\ncurrent, I, in amperes, A (amp is acceptable for ampere)\nresistance, R, in ohms, Ω\nMS 3b, c\nStudents should be able to\nrecall and apply this\nequation.\nRequired practical activity 15: use circuit diagrams to set up and check appropriate circuits to\ninvestigate the factors affecting the resistance of electrical circuits. This should include:\n• the length of a wire at constant temperature\n• combinations of resistors in series and parallel.\nAT skills covered by this practical activity: physics AT 1, 6 and 7.\nThis practical activity also provides opportunities to develop WS and MS. Details of all skills are\ngiven in Key opportunities for skills development (page 185).\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 129\n6.2.1.4 Resistors\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nStudents should be able to explain that, for some resistors, the\nvalue of R remains constant but that in others it can change as the\ncurrent changes.\nThe current through an ohmic conductor (at a constant\ntemperature) is directly proportional to the potential difference\nacross the resistor. This means that the resistance remains\nconstant as the current changes.\nThe resistance of components such as lamps, diodes, thermistors\nand LDRs is not constant; it changes with the current through the\ncomponent.\nThe resistance of a filament lamp increases as the temperature of\nthe filament increases.\nThe current through a diode flows in one direction only. The diode\nhas a very high resistance in the reverse direction.\n130 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThe resistance of a thermistor decreases as the temperature\nincreases.\nThe applications of thermistors in circuits eg a thermostat is\nrequired.\nThe resistance of an LDR decreases as light intensity increases.\nThe application of LDRs in circuits eg switching lights on when it\ngets dark is required.\nStudents should be able to:\nWS 1.2, 1.4\n• explain the design and use of a circuit to measure the\nresistance of a component by measuring the current through,\nand potential difference across, the component\n• draw an appropriate circuit diagram using correct circuit\nsymbols.\nAT 6\nInvestigate the relationship\nbetween the resistance of a\nthermistor and temperature.\nInvestigate the relationship\nbetween the resistance of\nan LDR and light intensity.\nStudents should be able to use graphs to explore whether circuit\nelements are linear or non-linear and relate the curves produced to\ntheir function and properties.\nWS 1.2, 1.4\nMS 4c, 4d, 4e\nRequired practical activity 16: use circuit diagrams to construct appropriate circuits to investigate\nthe I–V characteristics of a variety of circuit elements, including a filament lamp, a diode and a\nresistor at constant tempera"

GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. The size of the electric\ncurrent is the rate of flow of electrical charge. Charge flow, current\nand time are linked by the equation:\ncharge f low = current × time\nQ = I t\ncharge flow, Q, in coulombs, C\ncurrent, I, in amperes, A (amp is acceptable for ampere)\ntime, t, in seconds, s\nA current has the same value at any point in a single closed loop.\nMS 3b, c\nStudents should be able to\nrecall and apply this\nequation.\n6.2.1.3 Current, resistance and potential difference\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThe current (I) through a component depends on both the\nresistance (R) of the component and the potential difference (V)\nacross the component. The greater the resistance of the component\nthe smaller the current for a given potential difference (pd) across\nthe component.\nQuestions will be set using the term potential difference. Students\nwill gain credit for the correct use of either potential difference or\nvoltage.\nCurrent, potential difference or resistance can be calculated using\nthe equation:\npotential di f f erence = current × resistance\nV = I R\npotential difference, V, in volts, V\ncurrent, I, in amperes, A (amp is acceptable for ampere)\nresistance, R, in ohms, Ω\nMS 3b, c\nStudents should be able to\nrecall and apply this\nequation.\nRequired practical activity 15: use circuit diagrams to set up and check appropriate circuits to\ninvestigate the factors affecting the resistance of electrical circuits. This should include:\n• the length of a wire at constant temperature\n• combinations of resistors in series and parallel.\nAT skills covered by this practical activity: physics AT 1, 6 and 7.\nThis practical activity also provides opportunities to develop WS and MS. Details of all skills are\ngiven in Key opportunities for skills development (page 185).\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 129\n6.2.1.4 Resistors\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nStudents should be able to explain that, for some resistors, the\nvalue of R remains constant but that in others it can change as the\ncurrent changes.\nThe current through an ohmic conductor (at a constant\ntemperature) is directly proportional to the potential difference\nacross the resistor. This means that the resistance remains\nconstant as the current changes.\nThe resistance of components such as lamps, diodes, thermistors\nand LDRs is not constant; it changes with the current through the\ncomponent.\nThe resistance of a filament lamp increases as the temperature of\nthe filament increases.\nThe current through a diode flows in one direction only. The diode\nhas a very high resistance in the reverse direction.\n130 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThe resistance of a thermistor decreases as the temperature\nincreases.\nThe applications of thermistors in circuits eg a thermostat is\nrequired.\nThe resistance of an LDR decreases as light intensity increases.\nThe application of LDRs in circuits eg switching lights on when it\ngets dark is required.\nStudents should be able to:\nWS 1.2, 1.4\n• explain the design and use of a circuit to measure the\nresistance of a component by measuring the current through,\nand potential difference across, the component\n• draw an appropriate circuit diagram using correct circuit\nsymbols.\nAT 6\nInvestigate the relationship\nbetween the resistance of a\nthermistor and temperature.\nInvestigate the relationship\nbetween the resistance of\nan LDR and light intensity.\nStudents should be able to use graphs to explore whether circuit\nelements are linear or non-linear and relate the curves produced to\ntheir function and properties.\nWS 1.2, 1.4\nMS 4c, 4d, 4e\nRequired practical activity 16: use circuit diagrams to construct appropriate circuits to investigate\nthe I–V characteristics of a variety of circuit elements, including a filament lamp, a diode and a\nresistor at constant temperature.\nAT skills covered by this practical activity: physics AT 6 and 7.\nThis practical activity also provides opportunities to develop WS and MS. Details of all skills are\ngiven in Key opportunities for skills development (page 186).\n6.2.2 Series and parallel circuits\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThere are two ways of joining electrical components, in series and\nin parallel. Some circuits include both series and parallel parts.\nFor components connected in series:\n• there is the same current through each component\n• the total potential difference of the power supply is shared\nbetween the components\n• the total resistance of two components is the sum of the\nresistance of each component.\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 131\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nRtotal = R1 + R2\nresistance, R, in ohms, Ω\nFor components connected in parallel:\n• the potential difference across each component is the same\n• the total current through the whole circuit is the sum of the\ncurrents through the separate components\n• the total resistance of two resistors is less than the resistance\nof the smallest individual resistor.\nStudents should be able to:\nMS 1c, 3b, 3c, 3d\n• use circuit diagrams to construct and check series and\nparallel circuits that include a variety of common circuit\ncomponents\n• describe the difference between series and parallel circuits\n• explain qualitatively why adding resistors in series increases\nthe total resistance whilst adding resistors in parallel\ndecreases the total resistance\nAT 7\n• explain the design and use of dc series circuits for\nmeasurement and testing purposes\nWS 1.4\n• calculate the currents, potential differences and resistances in\ndc series circuits\n• solve problems for circuits which include resistors in series\nusing the concept of equivalent resistance.\nStudents are not required to calculate the total resistance of two\nresistors joined in parallel.\nMS 1c, 3b, c, d\n6.2.3 Domestic uses and safety\n6.2.3.1 Direct and alternating potential difference\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nMains electricity is an ac supply. In the United Kingdom the\ndomestic electricity supply has a frequency of 50 Hz and is about\n230 V.\nStudents should be able to explain the difference between direct\nand alternating potential difference.\n132 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up"

In the United Kingdom the\ndomestic electricity supply has a frequency of 50 Hz and is about\n230 V.\nStudents should be able to explain the difference between direct\nand alternating potential difference.\n132 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\n6.2.3.2 Mains electricity\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nMost electrical appliances are connected to the mains using three-\ncore cable.\nThe insulation covering each wire is colour coded for easy\nidentification:\nlive wire – brown\nneutral wire – blue\nearth wire – green and yellow stripes.\nThe live wire carries the alternating potential difference from the\nsupply. The neutral wire completes the circuit. The earth wire is a\nsafety wire to stop the appliance becoming live.\nThe potential difference between the live wire and earth (0 V) is\nabout 230 V. The neutral wire is at, or close to, earth potential (0 V).\nThe earth wire is at 0 V, it only carries a current if there is a fault.\nStudents should be able to explain:\n• that a live wire may be dangerous even when a switch in the\nmains circuit is open\n• the dangers of providing any connection between the live wire\nand earth.\nWS 1.5\n6.2.4 Energy transfers\n6.2.4.1 Power\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nStudents should be able to explain how the power transfer in any\ncircuit device is related to the potential difference across it and the\ncurrent through it, and to the energy changes over time:\npower = potential di f f erence × current\nP = V I\npower = current\n2\n× resistance\nP = I2\nR\npower, P, in watts, W\npotential difference, V, in volts, V\ncurrent, I, in amperes, A (amp is acceptable for ampere)\nresistance, R, in ohms, Ω\nMS 3b, c\nWS 4.5\nStudents should be able to\nrecall and apply both\nequations.\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. Charge flow, current\nand time are linked by the equation:\ncharge f low = current × time\nQ = I t\ncharge flow, Q, in coulombs, C\ncurrent, I, in amperes, A (amp is acceptable for ampere)\ntime, t, in seconds, s\nA current has the same value at any point in a single closed loop.\nMS 3b, c\nStudents should be able to\nrecall and apply this\nequation.\n6.2.1.3 Current, resistance and potential difference\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThe current (I) through a component depends on both the\nresistance (R) of the component and the potential difference (V)\nacross the component. The greater the resistance of the component\nthe smaller the current for a given potential difference (pd) across\nthe component.\nQuestions will be set using the term potential difference. Students\nwill gain credit for the correct use of either potential difference or\nvoltage.\nCurrent, potential difference or resistance can be calculated using\nthe equation:\npotential di f f erence = current × resistance\nV = I R\npotential difference, V, in volts, V\ncurrent, I, in amperes, A (amp is acceptable for ampere)\nresistance, R, in ohms, Ω\nMS 3b, c\nStudents should be able to\nrecall and apply this\nequation.\nRequired practical activity 15: use circuit diagrams to set up and check appropriate circuits to\ninvestigate the factors affecting the resistance of electrical circuits. This should include:\n• the length of a wire at constant temperature\n• combinations of resistors in series and parallel.\nAT skills covered by this practical activity: physics AT 1, 6 and 7.\nThis practical activity also provides opportunities to develop WS and MS. Details of all skills are\ngiven in Key opportunities for skills development (page 185).\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. This means that the resistance remains\nconstant as the current changes.\nThe resistance of components such as lamps, diodes, thermistors\nand LDRs is not constant; it changes with the current through the\ncomponent.\nThe resistance of a filament lamp increases as the temperature of\nthe filament increases.\nThe current through a diode flows in one direction only. The diode\nhas a very high resistance in the reverse direction.\n130 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThe resistance of a thermistor decreases as the temperature\nincreases.\nThe applications of thermistors in circuits eg a thermostat is\nrequired.\nThe resistance of an LDR decreases as light intensity increases.\nThe application of LDRs in circuits eg switching lights on when it\ngets dark is required.\nStudents should be able to:\nWS 1.2, 1.4\n• explain the design and use of a circuit to measure the\nresistance of a component by measuring the current through,\nand potential difference across, the component\n• draw an appropriate circuit diagram using correct circuit\nsymbols.\nAT 6\nInvestigate the relationship\nbetween the resistance of a\nthermistor and temperature.\nInvestigate the relationship\nbetween the resistance of\nan LDR and light intensity.\nStudents should be able to use graphs to explore whether circuit\nelements are linear or non-linear and relate the curves produced to\ntheir function and properties.\nWS 1.2, 1.4\nMS 4c, 4d, 4e\nRequired practical activity 16: use circuit diagrams to construct appropriate circuits to investigate\nthe I–V characteristics of a variety of circuit elements, including a filament lamp, a diode and a\nresistor at constant temperature.\nAT skills covered by this practical activity: physics AT 6 and 7.\nThis practical activity also provides opportunities to develop WS and MS. Details of all skills are\ngiven in Key opportunities for skills development (page 186).\n6.2.2 Series and parallel circuits\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThere are two ways of joining electrical components, in series and\nin parallel. Some circuits include both series and parallel parts.\nFor components connected in series:\n• there is the same current through each component\n• the total potential difference of the power supply is shared\nbetween the components\n• the total resistance of two components is the sum of the\nresistance of each component.\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards.           "locator": "Official PDF pages 135-138; 6.3 Particle model of matter",
          "text": "Official PDF pages 135-138\nParticle model of matter\nThe particle model is widely used to predict the behaviour of solids, liquids and gases and this has\nmany applications in everyday life. It helps us to explain a wide range of observations and\nengineers use these principles when designing vessels to withstand high pressures and\ntemperatures, such as submarines and spacecraft. It also explains why it is difficult to make a good\ncup of tea high up a mountain!\n6.3.1 Changes of state and the particle model\n6.3.1.1 Density of materials\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThe density of a material is defined by the equation:\ndensity = mass\nvolume\nρ =\nm\nV\ndensity, ρ, in kilograms per metre cubed, kg/m3\nmass, m, in kilograms, kg\nvolume, V, in metres cubed, m3\nThe particle model can be used to explain\n• the different states of matter\n• differences in density.\nMS 1a, b, c, 3b, c\nStudents should be able to\nrecall and apply this\nequation to changes where\nmass is conserved.\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 135\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nStudents should be able to recognise/draw simple diagrams to\nmodel the difference between solids, liquids and gases.\nWS 1.2\nStudents should be able to explain the differences in density\nbetween the different states of matter in terms of the arrangement\nof atoms or molecules.\nWS 1.2\nRequired practical activity 17: use appropriate apparatus to make and record the measurements\nneeded to determine the densities of regular and irregular solid objects and liquids. Volume should\nbe determined from the dimensions of regularly shaped objects, and by a displacement technique\nfor irregularly shaped objects. Dimensions to be measured using appropriate apparatus such as a\nruler, micrometer or Vernier callipers.\nAT skills covered by this practical activity: physics AT 1.\nThis practical activity also provides opportunities to develop WS and MS. Details of all skills are\ngiven in Key opportunities for skills development (page 187).\n6.3.1.2 Changes of state\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nStudents should be able to describe how, when substances change\nstate (melt, freeze, boil, evaporate, condense or sublimate), mass is\nconserved.\nChanges of state are physical changes which differ from chemical\nchanges because the material recovers its original properties if the\nchange is reversed.\n6.3.2 Internal energy and energy transfers\n6.3.2.1 Internal energy\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nEnergy is stored inside a system by the particles (atoms and\nmolecules) that make up the system. This is called internal energy.\nInternal energy is the total kinetic energy and potential energy of all\nthe particles (atoms and molecules) that make up a system.\nHeating changes the energy stored within the system by increasing\nthe energy of the particles that make up the system. This either\nraises the temperature of the system or produces a change of state.\n136 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\n6.3.2.2 Temperature changes in a system and specific heat capacity\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nIf the temperature of the system increases:\nThe increase in temperature depends on the mass of the substance\nheated, the type of material and the energy input to the system.\nThe following equation applies:\nchange in thermal energy = mass × speci f ic heat capacity\n× temperature change\n∆ E = m c ∆ θ\nchange in thermal energy, ∆E, in joules, J\nmass, m, in kilograms, kg\nspecific heat capacity, c, in joules per kilogram per degree Celsius,\nJ/kg °C\ntemperature change, ∆θ, in degrees Celsius, °C.\nThe specific heat capacity of a substance is the amount of energy\nrequired to raise the temperature of one kilogram of the substance\nby one degree Celsius.\nMS 1a, 3b, 3c, 3d\nStudents should be able to\napply this equation, which is\ngiven on the Physics\nequation sheet, to calculate\nthe energy change involved\nwhen the temperature of a\nmaterial changes.\nThis equation and specific\nheat capacity are also\nincluded in Energy changes\nin systems (page 123).\n6.3.2.3 Changes of state and specific latent heat\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nIf a change of state happens:\nThe energy needed for a substance to change state is called latent\nheat. When a change of state occurs, the energy supplied changes\nthe energy stored (internal energy) but not the temperature.\nThe specific latent heat of a substance is the amount of energy\nrequired to change the state of one kilogram of the substance with\nno change in temperature.\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 137\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nenergy f or a change o f state = mass × speci f ic latent heat\nE = m L\nenergy, E, in joules, J\nmass, m, in kilograms, kg\nspecific latent heat, L, in joules per kilogram, J/kg\nSpecific latent heat of fusion – change of state from solid to liquid\nSpecific latent heat of vaporisation – change of state from liquid to\nvapour\nMS 1a, 3b, c, d\nStudents should be able to\napply this equation, which is\ngiven on the Physics\nequation sheet, to calculate\nthe energy change involved\nin a change of state.\nMS 4a\nAT 5\nPerform an experiment to\nmeasure the latent heat of\nfusion of water.\nStudents should be able to interpret heating and cooling graphs that\ninclude changes of state.\nStudents should be able to distinguish between specific heat\ncapacity and specific latent heat.\nWS 3.5\n6.3.3 Particle model and pressure\n6.3.3.1 Particle motion in gases\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThe molecules of a gas are in constant random motion. The\ntemperature of the gas is related to the average kinetic energy of\nthe molecules.\nChanging the temperature of a gas, held at constant volume,\nchanges the pressure exerted by the gas.\nWS 1.2\nStudents should be able to:\n• explain how the motion of the molecules in a gas is related to\nboth its temperature and its pressure\n• explain qualitatively the relation between the temperature of a\ngas and its pressure at constant volume.\nWS 1.2\n6.4 Atomic structure\nIonising radiation is hazardous but can be very useful. Although radioactivity was discovered over\na century ago, it took many nuclear physicists several decades to understand the structure of\natoms, nuclear forces and stability. Early researchers suffered from their exposure to ionising\nradiation. Rules for radiological protection were first introduced in the 1930s and subsequently\n138 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration"

          "text": "Official PDF pages 135-138\nChanges of state and the particle model\n6.3.1.1 Density of materials\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThe density of a material is defined by the equation:\ndensity = mass\nvolume\nρ =\nm\nV\ndensity, ρ, in kilograms per metre cubed, kg/m3\nmass, m, in kilograms, kg\nvolume, V, in metres cubed, m3\nThe particle model can be used to explain\n• the different states of matter\n• differences in density.\nMS 1a, b, c, 3b, c\nStudents should be able to\nrecall and apply this\nequation to changes where\nmass is conserved.\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 135\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nStudents should be able to recognise/draw simple diagrams to\nmodel the difference between solids, liquids and gases.\nWS 1.2\nStudents should be able to explain the differences in density\nbetween the different states of matter in terms of the arrangement\nof atoms or molecules.\nWS 1.2\nRequired practical activity 17: use appropriate apparatus to make and record the measurements\nneeded to determine the densities of regular and irregular solid objects and liquids. Volume should\nbe determined from the dimensions of regularly shaped objects, and by a displacement technique\nfor irregularly shaped objects. Dimensions to be measured using appropriate apparatus such as a\nruler, micrometer or Vernier callipers.\nAT skills covered by this practical activity: physics AT 1.\nThis practical activity also provides opportunities to develop WS and MS. Details of all skills are\ngiven in Key opportunities for skills development (page 187).\n6.3.1.2 Changes of state\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nStudents should be able to describe how, when substances change\nstate (melt, freeze, boil, evaporate, condense or sublimate), mass is\nconserved.\nChanges of state are physical changes which differ from chemical\nchanges because the material recovers its original properties if the\nchange is reversed.\n6.3.2 Internal energy and energy transfers\n6.3.2.1 Internal energy\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nEnergy is stored inside a system by the particles (atoms and\nmolecules) that make up the system. This is called internal energy.\nInternal energy is the total kinetic energy and potential energy of all\nthe particles (atoms and molecules) that make up a system.\nHeating changes the energy stored within the system by increasing\nthe energy of the particles that make up the system. This either\nraises the temperature of the system or produces a change of state.\n136 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\n6.3.2.2 Temperature changes in a system and specific heat capacity\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nIf the temperature of the system increases:\nThe increase in temperature depends on the mass of the substance\nheated, the type of material and the energy input to the system.\nThe following equation applies:\nchange in thermal energy = mass × speci f ic heat capacity\n× temperature change\n∆ E = m c ∆ θ\nchange in thermal energy, ∆E, in joules, J\nmass, m, in kilograms, kg\nspecific heat capacity, c, in joules per kilogram per degree Celsius,\nJ/kg °C\ntemperature change, ∆θ, in degrees Celsius, °C.\nThe specific heat capacity of a substance is the amount of energy\nrequired to raise the temperature of one kilogram of the substance\nby one degree Celsius.\nMS 1a, 3b, 3c, 3d\nStudents should be able to\napply this equation, which is\ngiven on the Physics\nequation sheet, to calculate\nthe energy change involved\nwhen the temperature of a\nmaterial changes.\nThis equation and specific\nheat capacity are also\nincluded in Energy changes\nin systems (page 123).\n6.3.2.3 Changes of state and specific latent heat\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nIf a change of state happens:\nThe energy needed for a substance to change state is called latent\nheat. When a change of state occurs, the energy supplied changes\nthe energy stored (internal energy) but not the temperature.\nThe specific latent heat of a substance is the amount of energy\nrequired to change the state of one kilogram of the substance with\nno change in temperature.\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 137\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nenergy f or a change o f state = mass × speci f ic latent heat\nE = m L\nenergy, E, in joules, J\nmass, m, in kilograms, kg\nspecific latent heat, L, in joules per kilogram, J/kg\nSpecific latent heat of fusion – change of state from solid to liquid\nSpecific latent heat of vaporisation – change of state from liquid to\nvapour\nMS 1a, 3b, c, d\nStudents should be able to\napply this equation, which is\ngiven on the Physics\nequation sheet, to calculate\nthe energy change involved\nin a change of state.\nMS 4a\nAT 5\nPerform an experiment to\nmeasure the latent heat of\nfusion of water.\nStudents should be able to interpret heating and cooling graphs that\ninclude changes of state.\nStudents should be able to distinguish between specific heat\ncapacity and specific latent heat.\nWS 3.5\n6.3.3 Particle model and pressure\n6.3.3.1 Particle motion in gases\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThe molecules of a gas are in constant random motion. The\ntemperature of the gas is related to the average kinetic energy of\nthe molecules.\nChanging the temperature of a gas, held at constant volume,\nchanges the pressure exerted by the gas.\nWS 1.2\nStudents should be able to:\n• explain how the motion of the molecules in a gas is related to\nboth its temperature and its pressure\n• explain qualitatively the relation between the temperature of a\ngas and its pressure at constant volume.\nWS 1.2\n6.4 Atomic structure\nIonising radiation is hazardous but can be very useful. Although radioactivity was discovered over\na century ago, it took many nuclear physicists several decades to understand the structure of\natoms, nuclear forces and stability. Early researchers suffered from their exposure to ionising\nradiation. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 137\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nenergy f or a change o f state = mass × speci f ic latent heat\nE = m L\nenergy, E, in joules, J\nmass, m, in kilograms, kg\nspecific latent heat, L, in joules per kilogram, J/kg\nSpecific latent heat of fusion – change of state from solid to liquid\nSpecific latent heat of vaporisation – change of state from liquid to\nvapour\nMS 1a, 3b, c, d\nStudents should be able to\napply this equation, which is\ngiven on the Physics\nequation sheet, to calculate\nthe energy change involved\nin a change of state.\nMS 4a\nAT 5\nPerform an experiment to\nmeasure the latent heat of\nfusion of water.\nStudents should be able to interpret heating and cooling graphs that\ninclude changes of state.\nStudents should be able to distinguish between specific heat\ncapacity and specific latent heat.\nWS 3.5\n6.3.3 Particle model and pressure\n6.3.3.1 Particle motion in gases\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThe molecules of a gas are in constant random motion. The\ntemperature of the gas is related to the average kinetic energy of\nthe molecules.\nChanging the temperature of a gas, held at constant volume,\nchanges the pressure exerted by the gas.\nWS 1.2\nStudents should be able to:\n• explain how the motion of the molecules in a gas is related to\nboth its temperature and its pressure\n• explain qualitatively the relation between the temperature of a\ngas and its pressure at constant volume.\nWS 1.2\n6.4 Atomic structure\nIonising radiation is hazardous but can be very useful. Although radioactivity was discovered over\na century ago, it took many nuclear physicists several decades to understand the structure of\natoms, nuclear forces and stability. Early researchers suffered from their exposure to ionising\nradiation.           "locator": "Official PDF pages 138-143; 6.4 Atomic structure",
          "text": "Official PDF pages 138-143\nAtomic structure\nIonising radiation is hazardous but can be very useful. Although radioactivity was discovered over\na century ago, it took many nuclear physicists several decades to understand the structure of\natoms, nuclear forces and stability. Early researchers suffered from their exposure to ionising\nradiation. Rules for radiological protection were first introduced in the 1930s and subsequently\n138 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\nimproved. Today radioactive materials are widely used in medicine, industry, agriculture and\nelectrical power generation.\n6.4.1 Atoms and isotopes\n6.4.1.1 The structure of an atom\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nAtoms are very small, having a radius of about 1 × 10-10 metres.\nThe basic structure of an atom is a positively charged nucleus\ncomposed of both protons and neutrons surrounded by negatively\ncharged electrons.\nThe radius of a nucleus is less than 1/10 000 of the radius of an\natom. Most of the mass of an atom is concentrated in the nucleus.\nThe electrons are arranged at different distances from the nucleus\n(different energy levels). The electron arrangements may change\nwith the absorption of electromagnetic radiation (move further from\nthe nucleus; a higher energy level) or by the emission of\nelectromagnetic radiation (move closer to the nucleus; a lower\nenergy level).\nMS 1b\nWS 4.4\nStudents should be able to\nrecognise expressions\ngiven in standard form.\n6.4.1.2 Mass number, atomic number and isotopes\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nIn an atom the number of electrons is equal to the number of\nprotons in the nucleus. Atoms have no overall electrical charge.\nAll atoms of a particular element have the same number of protons.\nThe number of protons in an atom of an element is called its atomic\nnumber.\nThe total number of protons and neutrons in an atom is called its\nmass number.\nAtoms can be represented as shown in this example:\nAtoms of the same element can have different numbers of\nneutrons; these atoms are called isotopes of that element.\nAtoms turn into positive ions if they lose one or more outer\nelectron(s).\nStudents should be able to relate differences between isotopes to\ndifferences in conventional representations of their identities,\ncharges and masses.\nWS 4.1\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 139\n6.4.1.3 The development of the model of the atom (common content with chemistry)\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nNew experimental evidence may lead to a scientific model being\nchanged or replaced.\nBefore the discovery of the electron, atoms were thought to be tiny\nspheres that could not be divided.\nThe discovery of the electron led to the plum pudding model of the\natom. The plum pudding model suggested that the atom is a ball of\npositive charge with negative electrons embedded in it.\nThe results from the alpha particle scattering experiment led to the\nconclusion that the mass of an atom was concentrated at the centre\n(nucleus) and that the nucleus was charged. This nuclear model\nreplaced the plum pudding model.\nNiels Bohr adapted the nuclear model by suggesting that electrons\norbit the nucleus at specific distances. The theoretical calculations\nof Bohr agreed with experimental observations.\nLater experiments led to the idea that the positive charge of any\nnucleus could be subdivided into a whole number of smaller\nparticles, each particle having the same amount of positive charge.\nThe name proton was given to these particles.\nThe experimental work of James Chadwick provided the evidence\nto show the existence of neutrons within the nucleus. This was\nabout 20 years after the nucleus became an accepted scientific\nidea.\nStudents should be able to describe:\nWS 1.1, 1.6\nThis historical context\nprovides an opportunity for\nstudents to show an\nunderstanding of why and\ndescribe how scientific\nmethods and theories\ndevelop over time.\nWS 1.2\n• why the new evidence from the scattering experiment led to a\nchange in the atomic model\nWS 1.1\n• the difference between the plum pudding model of the atom\nand the nuclear model of the atom.\nWS 1.2\nDetails of experimental work supporting the Bohr model are not\nrequired.\nDetails of Chadwick’s experimental work are not required.\n140 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\n6.4.2 Atoms and nuclear radiation\n6.4.2.1 Radioactive decay and nuclear radiation\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nSome atomic nuclei are unstable. The nucleus gives out radiation\nas it changes to become more stable. This is a random process\ncalled radioactive decay.\nActivity is the rate at which a source of unstable nuclei decays.\nActivity is measured in becquerel (Bq)\nCount-rate is the number of decays recorded each second by a\ndetector (eg Geiger-Muller tube).\nThe nuclear radiation emitted may be:\n• an alpha particle (α) – this consists of two neutrons and two\nprotons, it is the same as a helium nucleus\n• a beta particle (β) – a high speed electron ejected from the\nnucleus as a neutron turns into a proton\n• a gamma ray (γ) – electromagnetic radiation from the nucleus\n• a neutron (n).\nRequired knowledge of the properties of alpha particles, beta\nparticles and gamma rays is limited to their penetration through\nmaterials, their range in air and ionising power.\nStudents should be able to apply their knowledge to the uses of\nradiation and evaluate the best sources of radiation to use in a\ngiven situation.\nWS 1.4, 1.5\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 141\n6.4.2.2 Nuclear equations\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nNuclear equations are used to represent radioactive decay.\nIn a nuclear equation an alpha particle may be represented by the\nsymbol:\nand a beta particle by the symbol:\nThe emission of the different types of nuclear radiation may cause a\nchange in the mass and /or the charge of the nucleus. For example:\nSo alpha decay causes both the mass and charge of the nucleus to\ndecrease.\nSo beta decay does not cause the mass of the nucleus to change\nbut does cause the charge of the nucleus to increase.\nStudents are not required to recall these two examples.\nStudents should be able to use the names and symbols of common\nnuclei and particles to write balanced equations that show single\nalpha (α) and beta (β) decay. This is limited to balancing the atomic\nnumbers and mass numbers. The identification of daughter\nelements from such decays is not required.\nThe emission of a gamma ray does not cause the mass or the\ncharge of the nucleus to change.\nWS 1.2, 4.1\nMS 1b, c, 3c\n6.4.2.3 Half-lives and the random nature of radioactive dec"

The plum pudding model suggested that the atom is a ball of\npositive charge with negative electrons embedded in it.\nThe results from the alpha particle scattering experiment led to the\nconclusion that the mass of an atom was concentrated at the centre\n(nucleus) and that the nucleus was charged. The theoretical calculations\nof Bohr agreed with experimental observations.\nLater experiments led to the idea that the positive charge of any\nnucleus could be subdivided into a whole number of smaller\nparticles, each particle having the same amount of positive charge.\nThe name proton was given to these particles.\nThe experimental work of James Chadwick provided the evidence\nto show the existence of neutrons within the nucleus. This was\nabout 20 years after the nucleus became an accepted scientific\nidea.\nStudents should be able to describe:\nWS 1.1, 1.6\nThis historical context\nprovides an opportunity for\nstudents to show an\nunderstanding of why and\ndescribe how scientific\nmethods and theories\ndevelop over time.\nWS 1.2\n• why the new evidence from the scattering experiment led to a\nchange in the atomic model\nWS 1.1\n• the difference between the plum pudding model of the atom\nand the nuclear model of the atom.\nWS 1.2\nDetails of experimental work supporting the Bohr model are not\nrequired.\nDetails of Chadwick’s experimental work are not required.\n140 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\n6.4.2 Atoms and nuclear radiation\n6.4.2.1 Radioactive decay and nuclear radiation\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nSome atomic nuclei are unstable. The nucleus gives out radiation\nas it changes to become more stable. This is a random process\ncalled radioactive decay.\nActivity is the rate at which a source of unstable nuclei decays.\nActivity is measured in becquerel (Bq)\nCount-rate is the number of decays recorded each second by a\ndetector (eg Geiger-Muller tube).\nThe nuclear radiation emitted may be:\n• an alpha particle (α) – this consists of two neutrons and two\nprotons, it is the same as a helium nucleus\n• a beta particle (β) – a high speed electron ejected from the\nnucleus as a neutron turns into a proton\n• a gamma ray (γ) – electromagnetic radiation from the nucleus\n• a neutron (n).\nRequired knowledge of the properties of alpha particles, beta\nparticles and gamma rays is limited to their penetration through\nmaterials, their range in air and ionising power.\nStudents should be able to apply their knowledge to the uses of\nradiation and evaluate the best sources of radiation to use in a\ngiven situation.\nWS 1.4, 1.5\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 141\n6.4.2.2 Nuclear equations\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nNuclear equations are used to represent radioactive decay.\nIn a nuclear equation an alpha particle may be represented by the\nsymbol:\nand a beta particle by the symbol:\nThe emission of the different types of nuclear radiation may cause a\nchange in the mass and /or the charge of the nucleus. For example:\nSo alpha decay causes both the mass and charge of the nucleus to\ndecrease.\nSo beta decay does not cause the mass of the nucleus to change\nbut does cause the charge of the nucleus to increase.\nStudents are not required to recall these two examples.\nStudents should be able to use the names and symbols of common\nnuclei and particles to write balanced equations that show single\nalpha (α) and beta (β) decay. This is limited to balancing the atomic\nnumbers and mass numbers. The identification of daughter\nelements from such decays is not required.\nThe emission of a gamma ray does not cause the mass or the\ncharge of the nucleus to change.\nWS 1.2, 4.1\nMS 1b, c, 3c\n6.4.2.3 Half-lives and the random nature of radioactive decay\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nRadioactive decay is random.\nThe half-life of a radioactive isotope is the time it takes for the\nnumber of nuclei of the isotope in a sample to halve, or the time it\ntakes for the count rate (or activity) from a sample containing the\nisotope to fall to half its initial level.\n142 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nStudents should be able to explain the concept of half-life and how\nit is related to the random nature of radioactive decay.\nWS 1.2\nStudents should be able to determine the half-li"

          "text": "Official PDF pages 138-143\nMass number, atomic number and isotopes\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nIn an atom the number of electrons is equal to the number of\nprotons in the nucleus. Atoms have no overall electrical charge.\nAll atoms of a particular element have the same number of protons.\nThe number of protons in an atom of an element is called its atomic\nnumber.\nThe total number of protons and neutrons in an atom is called its\nmass number.\nAtoms can be represented as shown in this example:\nAtoms of the same element can have different numbers of\nneutrons; these atoms are called isotopes of that element.\nAtoms turn into positive ions if they lose one or more outer\nelectron(s).\nStudents should be able to relate differences between isotopes to\ndifferences in conventional representations of their identities,\ncharges and masses.\nWS 4.1\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. The identification of daughter\nelements from such decays is not required.\nThe emission of a gamma ray does not cause the mass or the\ncharge of the nucleus to change.\nWS 1.2, 4.1\nMS 1b, c, 3c\n6.4.2.3 Half-lives and the random nature of radioactive decay\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nRadioactive decay is random.\nThe half-life of a radioactive isotope is the time it takes for the\nnumber of nuclei of the isotope in a sample to halve, or the time it\ntakes for the count rate (or activity) from a sample containing the\nisotope to fall to half its initial level.\n142 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nStudents should be able to explain the concept of half-life and how\nit is related to the random nature of radioactive decay.\nWS 1.2\nStudents should be able to determine the half-life of a radioactive\nisotope from given information.\nMS 4a\n(HT only) Students should be able to calculate the net decline,\nexpressed as a ratio, in a radioactive emission after a given number\nof half-lives.\n(HT only) MS 1c, 3d\n6.4.2.4 Radioactive contamination\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nRadioactive contamination is the unwanted presence of materials\ncontaining radioactive atoms on other materials. The hazard from\ncontamination is due to the decay of the contaminating atoms. The\ntype of radiation emitted affects the level of hazard.\nIrradiation is the process of exposing an object to nuclear radiation.\nThe irradiated object does not become radioactive.\nWS 1.5\nStudents should be able to compare the hazards associated with\ncontamination and irradiation.\nWS 1.5\nSuitable precautions must be taken to protect against any hazard\nthat the radioactive source used in the process of irradiation may\npresent.\nWS 1.5\nStudents should understand that it is important for the findings of\nstudies into the effects of radiation on humans to be published and\nshared with other scientists so that the findings can be checked by\npeer review.\nWS 1.6\n6.5 Forces\nEngineers analyse forces when designing a great variety of machines and instruments, from road\nbridges and fairground rides to atomic force microscopes. Anything mechanical can be analysed in\nthis way. Recent developments in artificial limbs use the analysis of forces to make movement\npossible.\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 143"

          "text": "Official PDF pages 143-155\nForces\nEngineers analyse forces when designing a great variety of machines and instruments, from road\nbridges and fairground rides to atomic force microscopes. Anything mechanical can be analysed in\nthis way. Recent developments in artificial limbs use the analysis of forces to make movement\npossible.\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 143\n6.5.1 Forces and their interactions\n6.5.1.1 Scalar and vector quantities\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nScalar quantities have magnitude only.\nVector quantities have magnitude and an associated direction.\nA vector quantity may be represented by an arrow. The length of\nthe arrow represents the magnitude, and the direction of the arrow\nthe direction of the vector quantity.\n6.5.1.2 Contact and non-contact forces\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nA force is a push or pull that acts on an object due to the interaction\nwith another object. All forces between objects are either:\n• contact forces – the objects are physically touching\n• non-contact forces – the objects are physically separated.\nExamples of contact forces include friction, air resistance, tension\nand normal contact force.\nExamples of non-contact forces are gravitational force, electrostatic\nforce and magnetic force.\nForce is a vector quantity.\nStudents should be able to describe the interaction between pairs\nof objects which produce a force on each object. The forces to be\nrepresented as vectors.\n6.5.1.3 Gravity\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nWeight is the force acting on an object due to gravity. The force of\ngravity close to the Earth is due to the gravitational field around the\nEarth.\nThe weight of an object depends on the gravitational field strength\nat the point where the object is.\n144 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThe weight of an object can be calculated using the equation:\nweight = mass × gravitational field strength\nW = m g\nweight, W, in newtons, N\nmass, m, in kilograms, kg\ngravitational field strength, g, in newtons per kilogram, N/kg (In any\ncalculation the value of the gravitational field strength (g) will be\ngiven.)\nThe weight of an object may be considered to act at a single point\nreferred to as the object’s ‘centre of mass’.\nMS 3b, c\nStudents should be able to\nrecall and apply this\nequation.\nThe weight of an object and the mass of an object are directly\nproportional.\nWeight is measured using a calibrated spring-balance (a\nnewtonmeter).\nMS 3a\nStudents should recognise\nand be able to use the\nsymbol for proportionality, ∝\n6.5.1.4 Resultant forces\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nA number of forces acting on an object may be replaced by a single\nforce that has the same effect as all the original forces acting\ntogether. This single force is called the resultant force.\nStudents should be able to calculate the resultant of two forces that\nact in a straight line.\n(HT only) Students should be able to:\n• describe examples of the forces acting on an isolated object\nor system\n• use free body diagrams to describe qualitatively examples\nwhere several forces lead to a resultant force on an object,\nincluding balanced forces when the resultant force is zero.\n(HT only) A single force can be resolved into two components\nacting at right angles to each other. The two component forces\ntogether have the same effect as the single force.\nWS 1.2\n(HT only) Students should be able to use vector diagrams to\nillustrate resolution of forces, equilibrium situations and determine\nthe resultant of two forces, to include both magnitude and direction\n(scale drawings only).\nMS 4a, 5a, b\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 145\n6.5.2 Work done and energy transfer\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nWhen a force causes an object to move through a distance work is\ndone on the object. So a force does work on an object when the\nforce causes a displacement of the object.\nThe work done by a force on an object can be calculated using the\nequation:\nwork done = force × distance\nmoved along the line of action of the force\nW = F s\nwork done, W, in joules, J\nforce, F, in newtons, N\ndistance, s, in metres\nMS 3b, c\nStudents should be able to\nrecall and apply this\nequation.\nOne joule of work is done when a force of one newton causes a\ndisplacement of one metre.\n1 joule = 1 newton-metre\nStudents should be able to describe the energy transfer involved\nwhen work is done.\nWS 4.5\nStudents should be able to convert between newton-metres and\njoules.\nWork done against the frictional forces acting on an object causes a\nrise in the temperature of the object.\nMS 1c\nWS 4.5\n6.5.3 Forces and elasticity\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nStudents should be able to:\n• give examples of the forces involved in stretching, bending or\ncompressing an object\n• explain why, to change the shape of an object (by stretching,\nbending or compressing), more than one force has to be\napplied – this is limited to stationary objects only\n• describe the difference between elastic deformation and\ninelastic deformation caused by stretching forces.\nThe extension of an elastic object, such as a spring, is directly\nproportional to the force applied, provided that the limit of\nproportionality is not exceeded.\n146 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nf orce = spring constant × extension\nF = k e\nforce, F, in newtons, N\nspring constant, k, in newtons per metre, N/m\nextension, e, in metres, m\nMS 3b, c, 4a\nStudents should be able to\nrecall and apply this\nequation.\nThis relationship also applies to the compression of an elastic\nobject, where ‘e’ would be the compression of the object.\nA force that stretches (or compresses) a spring does work and\nelastic potential energy is stored in the spring. Provided the spring\nis not inelastically deformed, the work done on the spring and the\nelastic potential energy stored are equal.\nStudents should be able to:\n• describe the difference between a linear and non-linear\nrelationship between force and extension\n• calculate a spring constant in linear cases\nMS 3b, c, 4a\n• interpret data from an investigation of the relationship\nbetween force and extension\nWS 3.5\n• calculate work done in stretching (or compressing) a spring\n(up to the limit of proportionality) using the equation:\nelastic potential energy = 0.5 × spring constant × extension\n2\nEe = 1\n2\nk e2\nMS 3c\nStudents should be able to\napply this equation which is\ngiven on the Physics\nequation sheet.\nThis equation is also given\nin Changes"

          "locator": "Official PDF pages 143-155; 6.5.1.2 Contact and non-contact forces",
          "text": "Official PDF pages 143-155\nContact and non-contact forces\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nA force is a push or pull that acts on an object due to the interaction\nwith another object. All forces between objects are either:\n• contact forces – the objects are physically touching\n• non-contact forces – the objects are physically separated.\nExamples of contact forces include friction, air resistance, tension\nand normal contact force.\nExamples of non-contact forces are gravitational force, electrostatic\nforce and magnetic force.\nForce is a vector quantity.\nStudents should be able to describe the interaction between pairs\nof objects which produce a force on each object. The forces to be\nrepresented as vectors.\n6.5.1.3 Gravity\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nWeight is the force acting on an object due to gravity. The force of\ngravity close to the Earth is due to the gravitational field around the\nEarth.\nThe weight of an object depends on the gravitational field strength\nat the point where the object is.\n144 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThe weight of an object can be calculated using the equation:\nweight = mass × gravitational field strength\nW = m g\nweight, W, in newtons, N\nmass, m, in kilograms, kg\ngravitational field strength, g, in newtons per kilogram, N/kg (In any\ncalculation the value of the gravitational field strength (g) will be\ngiven.)\nThe weight of an object may be considered to act at a single point\nreferred to as the object’s ‘centre of mass’.\nMS 3b, c\nStudents should be able to\nrecall and apply this\nequation.\nThe weight of an object and the mass of an object are directly\nproportional.\nWeight is measured using a calibrated spring-balance (a\nnewtonmeter).\nMS 3a\nStudents should recognise\nand be able to use the\nsymbol for proportionality, ∝\n6.5.1.4 Resultant forces\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nA number of forces acting on an object may be replaced by a single\nforce that has the same effect as all the original forces acting\ntogether. This single force is called the resultant force.\nStudents should be able to calculate the resultant of two forces that\nact in a straight line.\n(HT only) Students should be able to:\n• describe examples of the forces acting on an isolated object\nor system\n• use free body diagrams to describe qualitatively examples\nwhere several forces lead to a resultant force on an object,\nincluding balanced forces when the resultant force is zero.\n(HT only) A single force can be resolved into two components\nacting at right angles to each other. The two component forces\ntogether have the same effect as the single force.\nWS 1.2\n(HT only) Students should be able to use vector diagrams to\nillustrate resolution of forces, equilibrium situations and determine\nthe resultant of two forces, to include both magnitude and direction\n(scale drawings only).\nMS 4a, 5a, b\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 145\n6.5.2 Work done and energy transfer\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nWhen a force causes an object to move through a distance work is\ndone on the object. So a force does work on an object when the\nforce causes a displacement of the object.\nThe work done by a force on an object can be calculated using the\nequation:\nwork done = force × distance\nmoved along the line of action of the force\nW = F s\nwork done, W, in joules, J\nforce, F, in newtons, N\ndistance, s, in metres\nMS 3b, c\nStudents should be able to\nrecall and apply this\nequation.\nOne joule of work is done when a force of one newton causes a\ndisplacement of one metre.\n1 joule = 1 newton-metre\nStudents should be able to describe the energy transfer involved\nwhen work is done.\nWS 4.5\nStudents should be able to convert between newton-metres and\njoules.\nWork done against the frictional forces acting on an object causes a\nrise in the temperature of the object.\nMS 1c\nWS 4.5\n6.5.3 Forces and elasticity\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nStudents should be able to:\n• give examples of the forces involved in stretching, bending or\ncompressing an object\n• explain why, to change the shape of an object (by stretching,\nbending or compressing), more than one force has to be\napplied – this is limited to stationary objects only\n• describe the difference between elastic deformation and\ninelastic deformation caused by stretching forces.\nThe extension of an elastic object, such as a spring, is directly\nproportional to the force applied, provided that the limit of\nproportionality is not exceeded.\n146 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nf orce = spring constant × extension\nF = k e\nforce, F, in newtons, N\nspring constant, k, in newtons per metre, N/m\nextension, e, in metres, m\nMS 3b, c, 4a\nStudents should be able to\nrecall and apply this\nequation.\nThis relationship also applies to the compression of an elastic\nobject, where ‘e’ would be the compression of the object.\nA force that stretches (or compresses) a spring does work and\nelastic potential energy is stored in the spring. Provided the spring\nis not inelastically deformed, the work done on the spring and the\nelastic potential energy stored are equal.\nStudents should be able to:\n• describe the difference between a linear and non-linear\nrelationship between force and extension\n• calculate a spring constant in linear cases\nMS 3b, c, 4a\n• interpret data from an investigation of the relationship\nbetween force and extension\nWS 3.5\n• calculate work done in stretching (or compressing) a spring\n(up to the limit of proportionality) using the equation:\nelastic potential energy = 0.5 × spring constant × extension\n2\nEe = 1\n2\nk e2\nMS 3c\nStudents should be able to\napply this equation which is\ngiven on the Physics\nequation sheet.\nThis equation is also given\nin Changes in energy (page\n122).\nStudents should be able to calculate relevant values of stored\nenergy and energy transfers.\nMS 3c\nRequired practical activity 18: investigate the relationship between force and extension for a\nspring.\nAT skills covered by this practical activity: physics AT 1 and 2.\nThis practical activity also provides opportunities to develop WS and MS. Details of all skills are\ngiven in Key opportunities for skills development (page 188).\n6.5.4 Forces and motion\n6.5.4.1 Describing motion along a line\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. Velocity is\na vector quantity.\nStudents should be able to explain the vector–scalar distinction as it\napplies to displacement, distance, velocity and speed.\n(HT only) Students should be able to explain qualitatively, with\nexamples, that motion in a circle involves constant speed but\nchanging velocity.\n6.5.4.1.4 The distance–time relationship\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nIf an object moves along a straight line, the distance travelled can\nbe represented by a distance–time graph.\nThe speed of an object can be calculated from the gradient of its\ndistance–time graph.\n(HT only) If an object is accelerating, its speed at any particular time\ncan be determined by drawing a tangent and measuring the\ngradient of the distance–time graph at that time.\nStudents should be able to draw distance–time graphs from\nmeasurements and extract and interpret lines and slopes of\ndistance–time graphs, translating information between graphical\nand numerical form.\nStudents should be able to determine speed from a distance–time\ngraph.\nMS 4a, b, c, d, f\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy"
