          "text": "Official PDF pages 143-155\nForces and elasticity\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nStudents should be able to:\n• give examples of the forces involved in stretching, bending or\ncompressing an object\n• explain why, to change the shape of an object (by stretching,\nbending or compressing), more than one force has to be\napplied – this is limited to stationary objects only\n• describe the difference between elastic deformation and\ninelastic deformation caused by stretching forces.\nThe extension of an elastic object, such as a spring, is directly\nproportional to the force applied, provided that the limit of\nproportionality is not exceeded.\n146 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nf orce = spring constant × extension\nF = k e\nforce, F, in newtons, N\nspring constant, k, in newtons per metre, N/m\nextension, e, in metres, m\nMS 3b, c, 4a\nStudents should be able to\nrecall and apply this\nequation.\nThis relationship also applies to the compression of an elastic\nobject, where ‘e’ would be the compression of the object.\nA force that stretches (or compresses) a spring does work and\nelastic potential energy is stored in the spring. Provided the spring\nis not inelastically deformed, the work done on the spring and the\nelastic potential energy stored are equal.\nStudents should be able to:\n• describe the difference between a linear and non-linear\nrelationship between force and extension\n• calculate a spring constant in linear cases\nMS 3b, c, 4a\n• interpret data from an investigation of the relationship\nbetween force and extension\nWS 3.5\n• calculate work done in stretching (or compressing) a spring\n(up to the limit of proportionality) using the equation:\nelastic potential energy = 0.5 × spring constant × extension\n2\nEe = 1\n2\nk e2\nMS 3c\nStudents should be able to\napply this equation which is\ngiven on the Physics\nequation sheet.\nThis equation is also given\nin Changes in energy (page\n122).\nStudents should be able to calculate relevant values of stored\nenergy and energy transfers.\nMS 3c\nRequired practical activity 18: investigate the relationship between force and extension for a\nspring.\nAT skills covered by this practical activity: physics AT 1 and 2.\nThis practical activity also provides opportunities to develop WS and MS. Details of all skills are\ngiven in Key opportunities for skills development (page 188).\n6.5.4 Forces and motion\n6.5.4.1 Describing motion along a line\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 147\n6.5.4.1.1 Distance and displacement\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nDistance is how far an object moves. Distance does not involve\ndirection. Distance is a scalar quantity.\nDisplacement includes both the distance an object moves,\nmeasured in a straight line from the start point to the finish point\nand the direction of that straight line. Displacement is a vector\nquantity.\nStudents should be able to express a displacement in terms of both\nthe magnitude and direction.\nMS 1, 3c\nThroughout this section\n(Forces and motion),\nstudents should be able to\nuse ratios and proportional\nreasoning to convert units\nand to compute rates.\n6.5.4.1.2 Speed\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nSpeed does not involve direction. Speed is a scalar quantity.\nThe speed of a moving object is rarely constant. When people walk,\nrun or travel in a car their speed is constantly changing.\nThe speed at which a person can walk, run or cycle depends on\nmany factors including: age, terrain, fitness and distance travelled.\nTypical values may be taken as:\nwalking ̴ 1.5 m/s\nrunning ̴ 3 m/s\ncycling ̴ 6 m/s.\nStudents should be able to recall typical values of speed for a\nperson walking, running and cycling as well as the typical values of\nspeed for different types of transportation systems.\nIt is not only moving objects that have varying speed. The speed of\nsound and the speed of the wind also vary.\nA typical value for the speed of sound in air is 330 m/s.\nStudents should be able to make measurements of distance and\ntime and then calculate speeds of objects.\nFor an object moving at constant speed the distance travelled in a\nspecific time can be calculated using the equation:\nMS 1a, c, 2f\n148 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\ndistance travelled = speed × time\ns = v t\ndistance, s, in metres, m\nspeed, v, in metres per second, m/s\ntime, t, in seconds, s\nMS 3b, 3c\nStudents should be able to\nrecall and apply this\nequation.\nStudents should be able to calculate average speed for non-uniform\nmotion.\nMS 3b, 3c\n6.5.4.1.3 Velocity\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThe velocity of an object is its speed in a given direction. Velocity is\na vector quantity.\nStudents should be able to explain the vector–scalar distinction as it\napplies to displacement, distance, velocity and speed.\n(HT only) Students should be able to explain qualitatively, with\nexamples, that motion in a circle involves constant speed but\nchanging velocity.\n6.5.4.1.4 The distance–time relationship\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nIf an object moves along a straight line, the distance travelled can\nbe represented by a distance–time graph.\nThe speed of an object can be calculated from the gradient of its\ndistance–time graph.\n(HT only) If an object is accelerating, its speed at any particular time\ncan be determined by drawing a tangent and measuring the\ngradient of the distance–time graph at that time.\nStudents should be able to draw distance–time graphs from\nmeasurements and extract and interpret lines and slopes of\ndistance–time graphs, translating information between graphical\nand numerical form.\nStudents should be able to determine speed from a distance–time\ngraph.\nMS 4a, b, c, d, f\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 149\n6.5.4.1.5 Acceleration\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThe average acceleration of an object can be calculated using the\nequation:\nacceleration = change in velocity\ntime taken\na =\n∆ v\nt\nacceleration, a, in metres per second squared, m/s2\nchange in velocity, ∆v, in metres per second, m/s\ntime, t, in seconds, s\nAn object that slows down is decelerating.\nStudents should be able to estimate the magnitude of everyday\naccelerations.\nMS 1d, 3b, 3c\nStudents should be able to\nrecall and apply this\nequation.\nThe acceleration of an object can be calculated from the gradient of\na velocity–time graph.\n(HT only) The distance travelled by an object (or displacement of an\nobject) can be calculated from the area under a velocity–time\ngraph.\nStudents should be able to:\nMS 4"

opportunities for\nskills development\nAn object falling through a fluid initially accelerates due to the force\nof gravity. Eventually the resultant force will be zero and the object\nwill move at its terminal velocity.\n6.5.4.2 Forces, accelerations and Newton's Laws of motion\n6.5.4.2.1 Newton's First Law\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nNewton’s First Law:\nIf the resultant force acting on an object is zero and:\n• the object is stationary, the object remains stationary\n• the object is moving, the object continues to move at the\nsame speed and in the same direction. So the object\ncontinues to move at the same velocity.\nSo, when a vehicle travels at a steady speed the resistive forces\nbalance the driving force.\nSo, the velocity (speed and/or direction) of an object will only\nchange if a resultant force is acting on the object.\nStudents should be able to apply Newton’s First Law to explain the\nmotion of objects moving with a uniform velocity and objects where\nthe speed and/or direction changes.\n(HT only) The tendency of objects to continue in their state of rest\nor of uniform motion is called inertia.\n6.5.4.2.2 Newton's Second Law\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nNewton’s Second Law:\nThe acceleration of an object is proportional to the resultant force\nacting on the object, and inversely proportional to the mass of the\nobject.\nAs an equation:\nMS 3a\nStudents should recognise\nand be able to use the\nsymbol for p"

          "text": "Official PDF pages 143-155\nSpeed\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nSpeed does not involve direction. Speed is a scalar quantity.\nThe speed of a moving object is rarely constant. When people walk,\nrun or travel in a car their speed is constantly changing.\nThe speed at which a person can walk, run or cycle depends on\nmany factors including: age, terrain, fitness and distance travelled.\nTypical values may be taken as:\nwalking ̴ 1.5 m/s\nrunning ̴ 3 m/s\ncycling ̴ 6 m/s.\nStudents should be able to recall typical values of speed for a\nperson walking, running and cycling as well as the typical values of\nspeed for different types of transportation systems.\nIt is not only moving objects that have varying speed. The speed of\nsound and the speed of the wind also vary.\nA typical value for the speed of sound in air is 330 m/s.\nStudents should be able to make measurements of distance and\ntime and then calculate speeds of objects.\nFor an object moving at constant speed the distance travelled in a\nspecific time can be calculated using the equation:\nMS 1a, c, 2f\n148 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\ndistance travelled = speed × time\ns = v t\ndistance, s, in metres, m\nspeed, v, in metres per second, m/s\ntime, t, in seconds, s\nMS 3b, 3c\nStudents should be able to\nrecall and apply this\nequation.\nStudents should be able to calculate average speed for non-uniform\nmotion.\nMS 3b, 3c\n6.5.4.1.3 Velocity\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThe velocity of an object is its speed in a given direction. Velocity is\na vector quantity.\nStudents should be able to explain the vector–scalar distinction as it\napplies to displacement, distance, velocity and speed.\n(HT only) Students should be able to explain qualitatively, with\nexamples, that motion in a circle involves constant speed but\nchanging velocity.\n6.5.4.1.4 The distance–time relationship\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nIf an object moves along a straight line, the distance travelled can\nbe represented by a distance–time graph.\nThe speed of an object can be calculated from the gradient of its\ndistance–time graph.\n(HT only) If an object is accelerating, its speed at any particular time\ncan be determined by drawing a tangent and measuring the\ngradient of the distance–time graph at that time.\nStudents should be able to draw distance–time graphs from\nmeasurements and extract and interpret lines and slopes of\ndistance–time graphs, translating information between graphical\nand numerical form.\nStudents should be able to determine speed from a distance–time\ngraph.\nMS 4a, b, c, d, f\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 149\n6.5.4.1.5 Acceleration\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThe average acceleration of an object can be calculated using the\nequation:\nacceleration = change in velocity\ntime taken\na =\n∆ v\nt\nacceleration, a, in metres per second squared, m/s2\nchange in velocity, ∆v, in metres per second, m/s\ntime, t, in seconds, s\nAn object that slows down is decelerating.\nStudents should be able to estimate the magnitude of everyday\naccelerations.\nMS 1d, 3b, 3c\nStudents should be able to\nrecall and apply this\nequation.\nThe acceleration of an object can be calculated from the gradient of\na velocity–time graph.\n(HT only) The distance travelled by an object (or displacement of an\nobject) can be calculated from the area under a velocity–time\ngraph.\nStudents should be able to:\nMS 4a, b, c, d, f\n• draw velocity–time graphs from measurements and interpret\nlines and slopes to determine acceleration\n• (HT only) interpret enclosed areas in velocity–time graphs to\ndetermine distance travelled (or displacement)\nWS 3.3\n• (HT only) measure, when appropriate, the area under a\nvelocity–time graph by counting squares.\nWS 3.3\nThe following equation applies to uniform acceleration:\nf inal velocity\n2\n− initial velocity\n2\n= 2 × acceleration × distance\nv2\n− u2\n= 2 a s\nfinal velocity, v, in metres per second, m/s\ninitial velocity, u, in metres per second, m/s\nacceleration, a, in metres per second squared, m/s2\ndistance, s, in metres, m\nNear the Earth’s surface any object falling freely under gravity has\nan acceleration of about 9.8 m/s2.\nMS 3b, 3c\nStudents should be able to\napply this equation which is\ngiven on the Physics\nequation sheet.\n150 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nAn object falling through a fluid initially accelerates due to the force\nof gravity. Eventually the resultant force will be zero and the object\nwill move at its terminal velocity.\n6.5.4.2 Forces, accelerations and Newton's Laws of motion\n6.5.4.2.1 Newton's First Law\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nNewton’s First Law:\nIf the resultant force acting on an object is zero and:\n• the object is stationary, the object remains stationary\n• the object is moving, the object continues to move at the\nsame speed and in the same direction. So the object\ncontinues to move at the same velocity.\nSo, when a vehicle travels at a steady speed the resistive forces\nbalance the driving force.\nSo, the velocity (speed and/or direction) of an object will only\nchange if a resultant force is acting on the object.\nStudents should be able to apply Newton’s First Law to explain the\nmotion of objects moving with a uniform velocity and objects where\nthe speed and/or direction changes.\n(HT only) The tendency of objects to continue in their state of rest\nor of uniform motion is called inertia.\n6.5.4.2.2 Newton's Second Law\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nNewton’s Second Law:\nThe acceleration of an object is proportional to the resultant force\nacting on the object, and inversely proportional to the mass of the\nobject.\nAs an equation:\nMS 3a\nStudents should recognise\nand be able to use the\nsymbol for proportionality, ∝\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 151\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nresultant f orce = mass × acceleration\nF = m a\nforce, F, in newtons, N\nmass, m, in kilograms, kg\nacceleration, a, in metres per second squared, m/s2\nMS 3b, c\nWS 4.2\nStudents should be able to\nrecall and apply this\nequation.\n(HT only) Students should be able to explain that:\n• inertial mass is a measure of how difficult it is to change the\nvelocity of an object\n• inertial mass is defined as the ratio of force over acceleration.\nMS 3a\nStudents should be able to estimate the speed, accelerations and\nforces involved in large accelerations for everyday road transport.\nStudents should recognise and be able to use the symbol that\nindicates an ap"

Poor\ncondition of the vehicle is limited to the vehicle's brakes or tyres.\nStudents should be able to:\n• explain the factors which affect the distance required for road\ntransport vehicles to come to rest in emergencies, and the\nimplications for safety\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464.           "locator": "Official PDF pages 143-155; 6.5.4.2 Forces, accelerations and Newton's Laws of motion",
          "text": "Official PDF pages 143-155\nForces, accelerations and Newton's Laws of motion\n6.5.4.2.1 Newton's First Law\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nNewton’s First Law:\nIf the resultant force acting on an object is zero and:\n• the object is stationary, the object remains stationary\n• the object is moving, the object continues to move at the\nsame speed and in the same direction. So the object\ncontinues to move at the same velocity.\nSo, when a vehicle travels at a steady speed the resistive forces\nbalance the driving force.\nSo, the velocity (speed and/or direction) of an object will only\nchange if a resultant force is acting on the object.\nStudents should be able to apply Newton’s First Law to explain the\nmotion of objects moving with a uniform velocity and objects where\nthe speed and/or direction changes.\n(HT only) The tendency of objects to continue in their state of rest\nor of uniform motion is called inertia.\n6.5.4.2.2 Newton's Second Law\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nNewton’s Second Law:\nThe acceleration of an object is proportional to the resultant force\nacting on the object, and inversely proportional to the mass of the\nobject.\nAs an equation:\nMS 3a\nStudents should recognise\nand be able to use the\nsymbol for proportionality, ∝\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 151\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nresultant f orce = mass × acceleration\nF = m a\nforce, F, in newtons, N\nmass, m, in kilograms, kg\nacceleration, a, in metres per second squared, m/s2\nMS 3b, c\nWS 4.2\nStudents should be able to\nrecall and apply this\nequation.\n(HT only) Students should be able to explain that:\n• inertial mass is a measure of how difficult it is to change the\nvelocity of an object\n• inertial mass is defined as the ratio of force over acceleration.\nMS 3a\nStudents should be able to estimate the speed, accelerations and\nforces involved in large accelerations for everyday road transport.\nStudents should recognise and be able to use the symbol that\nindicates an approximate value or approximate answer, ̴\nMS 1d\nRequired practical activity 19: investigate the effect of varying the force on the acceleration of an\nobject of constant mass, and the effect of varying the mass of an object on the acceleration\nproduced by a constant force.\nAT skills covered by this practical activity: physics AT 1, 2 and 3.\nThis practical activity also provides opportunities to develop WS and MS. Details of all skills are\ngiven in Key opportunities for skills development (page 189).\n6.5.4.2.3 Newton's Third Law\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nNewton’s Third Law:\nWhenever two objects interact, the forces they exert on each other\nare equal and opposite.\nStudents should be able to apply Newton’s Third Law to examples\nof equilibrium situations.\nWS 1.2\n6.5.4.3 Forces and braking\n152 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\n6.5.4.3.1 Stopping distance\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThe stopping distance of a vehicle is the sum of the distance the\nvehicle travels during the driver’s reaction time (thinking distance)\nand the distance it travels under the braking force (braking\ndistance). For a given braking force the greater the speed of the\nvehicle, the greater the stopping distance.\n6.5.4.3.2 Reaction time\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nReaction times vary from person to person. Typical values range\nfrom 0.2 s to 0.9 s.\nA driver’s reaction time can be affected by tiredness, drugs and\nalcohol. Distractions may also affect a driver’s ability to react.\nStudents should be able to:\n• explain methods used to measure human reaction times and\nrecall typical results\n• interpret and evaluate measurements from simple methods to\nmeasure the different reaction times of students\nWS 3.5, 3.7\n• evaluate the effect of various factors on thinking distance\nbased on given data.\nWS 1.5, 2.2\nMS 1a, c\nAT 1\nMeasure the effect of\ndistractions on reaction\ntime.\n6.5.4.3.3 Factors affecting braking distance 1\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThe braking distance of a vehicle can be affected by adverse road\nand weather conditions and poor condition of the vehicle.\nAdverse road conditions include wet or icy conditions. Poor\ncondition of the vehicle is limited to the vehicle's brakes or tyres.\nStudents should be able to:\n• explain the factors which affect the distance required for road\ntransport vehicles to come to rest in emergencies, and the\nimplications for safety\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 153\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\n• estimate how the distance required for road vehicles to stop in\nan emergency varies over a range of typical speeds.\nMS 1c, 1d, 2c, 2d, 2f, 2h,\n3b, 3c\n6.5.4.3.4 Factors affecting braking distance 2\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nWhen a force is applied to the brakes of a vehicle, work done by the\nfriction force between the brakes and the wheel reduces the kinetic\nenergy of the vehicle and the temperature of the brakes increases.\nThe greater the speed of a vehicle the greater the braking force\nneeded to stop the vehicle in a certain distance.\nThe greater the braking force the greater the deceleration of the\nvehicle. Large decelerations may lead to brakes overheating and/or\nloss of control.\nStudents should be able to:\n• explain the dangers caused by large decelerations WS 1.5\n• (HT only) estimate the forces involved in the deceleration of\nroad vehicles in typical situations on a public road.\n(HT only) MS 1d\n6.5.5 Momentum (HT only)\n6.5.5.1 Momentum is a property of moving objects\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nMomentum is defined by the equation:\nmomentum = mass × velocity\np = m v\nmomentum, p, in kilograms metre per second, kg m/s\nmass, m, in kilograms, kg\nvelocity, v, in metres per second, m/s\nWS 1.2\nMS 3b, c\nStudents should be able to\nrecall and apply this\nequation.\n154 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\n6.5.5.2 Conservation of momentum\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nIn a closed system, the total momentum before an event is equal to\nthe total momentum after the event.\nThis is called conservation of momentum.\nStudents should be able to use the concept of momentum as a\nmodel to describe and explain examples of momentum in an event,\nsuch as a collision.\nAT 1, 2, 3\nInvestigate collisions\nbetween laboratory trollies\nusing light gates, data\nloggers or ticker timers to\nmeasure and record data.\n6.6 Waves\nWave behaviour is common in both natural and man-made systems. Waves carry energy from one\nplace to another and can also carry information."

Designing comfortable and safe structures such\nas bridges, houses and music performance halls requires an understanding of mechanical waves.\nModern technologies such as imaging and communication systems show how we can make the\nmost of electromagnetic waves.\n6.6.1 Waves in air, fluids and solids\n6.6.1.1 Transverse and longitudinal waves\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nWaves may be either transverse or longitudinal.\nThe ripples on a water surface are an example of a transverse\nwave.\nLongitudinal waves show areas of compression and rarefaction.\nSound waves travelling through air are longitudinal.\nStudents should be able to describe the difference between\nlongitudinal and transverse waves.\nWS 1.2\nStudents should be able to describe evidence that, for both ripples\non a water surface and sound waves in air, it is the wave and not\nthe water or air itself that travels.\nWS 1.2, 2.2\nAQA GCSE C"

Typical values range\nfrom 0.2 s to 0.9 s.\nA driver’s reaction time can be affected by tiredness, drugs and\nalcohol. Distractions may also affect a driver’s ability to react.\nStudents should be able to:\n• explain methods used to measure human reaction times and\nrecall typical results\n• interpret and evaluate measurements from simple methods to\nmeasure the different reaction times of students\nWS 3.5, 3.7\n• evaluate the effect of various factors on thinking distance\nbased on given data.\nWS 1.5, 2.2\nMS 1a, c\nAT 1\nMeasure the effect of\ndistractions on reaction\ntime.\n6.5.4.3.3 Factors affecting braking distance 1\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThe braking distance of a vehicle can be affected by adverse road\nand weather conditions and poor condition of the vehicle.\nAdverse road conditions include wet or icy conditions. Poor\ncondition of the vehicle is limited to the vehicle's brakes or tyres.\nStudents should be able to:\n• explain the factors which affect the distance required for road\ntransport vehicles to come to rest in emergencies, and the\nimplications for safety\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 153\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\n• estimate how the distance required for road vehicles to stop in\nan emergency varies over a range of typical speeds.\nMS 1c, 1d, 2c, 2d, 2f, 2h,\n3b, 3c\n6.5.4.3.4 Factors affecting braking distance 2\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nWhen a force is applied to the brakes of a vehicle, work done by the\nfriction force between the brakes and the wheel reduces the kinetic\nenergy of the vehicle and the temperature of the brakes increases.\nThe greater the speed of a vehicle the greater the braking force\nneeded to stop the vehicle in a certain distance.\nThe greater the braking force the greater the deceleration of the\nvehicle. Large decelerations may lead to brakes overheating and/or\nloss of control.\nStudents should be able to:\n• explain the dangers caused by large decelerations WS 1.5\n• (HT only) estimate the forces involved in the deceleration of\nroad vehicles in typical situations on a public road.\n(HT only) MS 1d\n6.5.5 Momentum (HT only)\n6.5.5.1 Momentum is a property of moving objects\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nMomentum is defined by the equation:\nmomentum = mass × velocity\np = m v\nmomentum, p, in kilograms metre per second, kg m/s\nmass, m, in kilograms, kg\nvelocity, v, in metres per second, m/s\nWS 1.2\nMS 3b, c\nStudents should be able to\nrecall and apply this\nequation.\n154 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\n6.5.5.2 Conservation of momentum\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nIn a closed system, the total momentum before an event is equal to\nthe total momentum after the event.\nThis is called conservation of momentum.\nStudents should be able to use the concept of momentum as a\nmodel to describe and explain examples of momentum in an event,\nsuch as a collision.\nAT 1, 2, 3\nInvestigate collisions\nbetween laboratory trollies\nusing light gates, data\nloggers or ticker timers to\nmeasure and record data.\n6.6 Waves\nWave behaviour is common in both natural and man-made systems. Waves carry energy from one\nplace to another and can also carry information. Designing comfortable and safe structures such\nas bridges, houses and music performance halls requires an understanding of mechanical waves.\nModern technologies such as imaging and communication systems show how we can make the\nmost of electromagnetic waves.\n6.6.1 Waves in air, fluids and solids\n6.6.1.1 Transverse and longitudinal waves\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nWaves may be either transverse or longitudinal.\nThe ripples on a water surface are an example of a transverse\nwave.\nLongitudinal waves show areas of compression and rarefaction.\nSound waves travelling through air are longitudinal.\nStudents should be able to describe the difference between\nlongitudinal and transverse waves.\nWS 1.2\nStudents should be able to describe evidence that, for both ripples\non a water surface and sound waves in air, it is the wave and not\nthe water or air itself that travels.\nWS 1.2, 2.2\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 155"

          "text": "Official PDF pages 143-155\nStopping distance\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThe stopping distance of a vehicle is the sum of the distance the\nvehicle travels during the driver’s reaction time (thinking distance)\nand the distance it travels under the braking force (braking\ndistance). For a given braking force the greater the speed of the\nvehicle, the greater the stopping distance.\n6.5.4.3.2 Reaction time\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nReaction times vary from person to person. Typical values range\nfrom 0.2 s to 0.9 s.\nA driver’s reaction time can be affected by tiredness, drugs and\nalcohol. Distractions may also affect a driver’s ability to react.\nStudents should be able to:\n• explain methods used to measure human reaction times and\nrecall typical results\n• interpret and evaluate measurements from simple methods to\nmeasure the different reaction times of students\nWS 3.5, 3.7\n• evaluate the effect of various factors on thinking distance\nbased on given data.\nWS 1.5, 2.2\nMS 1a, c\nAT 1\nMeasure the effect of\ndistractions on reaction\ntime.\n6.5.4.3.3 Factors affecting braking distance 1\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThe braking distance of a vehicle can be affected by adverse road\nand weather conditions and poor condition of the vehicle.\nAdverse road conditions include wet or icy conditions. Poor\ncondition of the vehicle is limited to the vehicle's brakes or tyres.\nStudents should be able to:\n• explain the factors which affect the distance required for road\ntransport vehicles to come to rest in emergencies, and the\nimplications for safety\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 153\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\n• estimate how the distance required for road vehicles to stop in\nan emergency varies over a range of typical speeds.\nMS 1c, 1d, 2c, 2d, 2f, 2h,\n3b, 3c\n6.5.4.3.4 Factors affecting braking distance 2\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nWhen a force is applied to the brakes of a vehicle, work done by the\nfriction force between the brakes and the wheel reduces the kinetic\nenergy of the vehicle and the temperature of the brakes increases.\nThe greater the speed of a vehicle the greater the braking force\nneeded to stop the vehicle in a certain distance.\nThe greater the braking force the greater the deceleration of the\nvehicle. Large decelerations may lead to brakes overheating and/or\nloss of control.\nStudents should be able to:\n• explain the dangers caused by large decelerations WS 1.5\n• (HT only) estimate the forces involved in the deceleration of\nroad vehicles in typical situations on a public road.\n(HT only) MS 1d\n6.5.5 Momentum (HT only)\n6.5.5.1 Momentum is a property of moving objects\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nMomentum is defined by the equation:\nmomentum = mass × velocity\np = m v\nmomentum, p, in kilograms metre per second, kg m/s\nmass, m, in kilograms, kg\nvelocity, v, in metres per second, m/s\nWS 1.2\nMS 3b, c\nStudents should be able to\nrecall and apply this\nequation.\n154 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\n6.5.5.2 Conservation of momentum\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nIn a closed system, the total momentum before an event is equal to\nthe total momentum after the event.\nThis is called conservation of momentum.\nStudents should be able to use the concept of momentum as a\nmodel to describe and explain examples of momentum in an event,\nsuch as a collision.\nAT 1, 2, 3\nInvestigate collisions\nbetween laboratory trollies\nusing light gates, data\nloggers or ticker timers to\nmeasure and record data.\n6.6 Waves\nWave behaviour is common in both natural and man-made systems. Waves carry energy from one\nplace to another and can also carry information. Designing comfortable and safe structures such\nas bridges, houses and music performance halls requires an understanding of mechanical waves.\nModern technologies such as imaging and communication systems show how we can make the\nmost of electromagnetic waves.\n6.6.1 Waves in air, fluids and solids\n6.6.1.1 Transverse and longitudinal waves\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nWaves may be either transverse or longitudinal.\nThe ripples on a water surface are an example of a transverse\nwave.\nLongitudinal waves show areas of compression and rarefaction.\nSound waves travelling through air are longitudinal.\nStudents should be able to describe the difference between\nlongitudinal and transverse waves.\nWS 1.2\nStudents should be able to describe evidence that, for both ripples\non a water surface and sound waves in air, it is the wave and not\nthe water or air itself that travels.\nWS 1.2, 2.2\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. Designing comfortable and safe structures such\nas bridges, houses and music performance halls requires an understanding of mechanical waves.\nModern technologies such as imaging and communication systems show how we can make the\nmost of electromagnetic waves.\n6.6.1 Waves in air, fluids and solids\n6.6.1.1 Transverse and longitudinal waves\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nWaves may be either transverse or longitudinal.\nThe ripples on a water surface are an example of a transverse\nwave.\nLongitudinal waves show areas of compression and rarefaction.\nSound waves travelling through air are longitudinal.\nStudents should be able to describe the difference between\nlongitudinal and transverse waves.\nWS 1.2\nStudents should be able to describe evidence that, for both ripples\non a water surface and sound waves in air, it is the wave and not\nthe water or air itself that travels.\nWS 1.2, 2.2\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 155"

          "text": "Official PDF pages 155-158\nWaves\nWave behaviour is common in both natural and man-made systems. Waves carry energy from one\nplace to another and can also carry information. Designing comfortable and safe structures such\nas bridges, houses and music performance halls requires an understanding of mechanical waves.\nModern technologies such as imaging and communication systems show how we can make the\nmost of electromagnetic waves.\n6.6.1 Waves in air, fluids and solids\n6.6.1.1 Transverse and longitudinal waves\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nWaves may be either transverse or longitudinal.\nThe ripples on a water surface are an example of a transverse\nwave.\nLongitudinal waves show areas of compression and rarefaction.\nSound waves travelling through air are longitudinal.\nStudents should be able to describe the difference between\nlongitudinal and transverse waves.\nWS 1.2\nStudents should be able to describe evidence that, for both ripples\non a water surface and sound waves in air, it is the wave and not\nthe water or air itself that travels.\nWS 1.2, 2.2\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 155\n6.6.1.2 Properties of waves\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nStudents should be able to describe wave motion in terms of their\namplitude, wavelength, frequency and period.\nThe amplitude of a wave is the maximum displacement of a point\non a wave away from its undisturbed position.\nThe wavelength of a wave is the distance from a point on one wave\nto the equivalent point on the adjacent wave.\nThe frequency of a wave is the number of waves passing a point\neach second.\nMS 1c, 3b, c\nperiod = 1\nf requency\nT = 1\nf\nperiod, T, in seconds, s\nfrequency, f, in hertz, Hz\nThe wave speed is the speed at which the energy is transferred (or\nthe wave moves) through the medium.\nAll waves obey the wave equation:\nMS 1c, 3b, c\nStudents should be able to\napply this equation which is\ngiven on the Physics\nequation sheet.\nwave speed = f requency × wavelength\nv = f λ\nwave speed, v, in metres per second, m/s\nfrequency, f, in hertz, Hz\nwavelength, λ, in metres, m\nStudents should be able to:\n• identify amplitude and wavelength from given diagrams\nMS 1c, 3b, 3c\nStudents should be able to\nrecall and apply this\nequation.\n• describe a method to measure the speed of sound waves in\nair\nAT 1\nWS 2.3, 2.4, 2.6, 2.7, 3.1,\n3.5\n• describe a method to measure the speed of ripples on a water\nsurface.\nAT 1, AT 4\nWS 2.3, 2.4, 2.6, 2.7, 3.1,\n3.5\nRequired practical activity 20: make observations to identify the suitability of apparatus to\nmeasure the frequency, wavelength and speed of waves in a ripple tank and waves in a solid and\ntake appropriate measurements.\nAT skills covered by this practical activity: physics AT 4.\n156 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\nThis practical activity also provides opportunities to develop WS and MS. Details of all skills are\ngiven in Key opportunities for skills development (page 191).\n6.6.2 Electromagnetic waves\n6.6.2.1 Types of electromagnetic waves\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nElectromagnetic waves are transverse waves that transfer energy\nfrom the source of the waves to an absorber.\nElectromagnetic waves form a continuous spectrum and all types of\nelectromagnetic wave travel at the same velocity through a vacuum\n(space) or air.\nThe waves that form the electromagnetic spectrum are grouped in\nterms of their wavelength and their frequency. Going from long to\nshort wavelength (or from low to high frequency) the groups are:\nradio, microwave, infrared, visible light (red to violet), ultraviolet, X-\nrays and gamma rays.\nOur eyes only detect visible light and so detect a limited range of\nelectromagnetic waves.\nStudents should be able to give examples that illustrate the transfer\nof energy by electromagnetic waves.\n6.6.2.2 Properties of electromagnetic waves 1\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\n(HT only) Different substances may absorb, transmit, refract or\nreflect electromagnetic waves in ways that vary with wavelength.\n(HT only) Some effects, for example refraction, are due to the\ndifference in velocity of the waves in different substances.\nStudents should be able to construct ray diagrams to illustrate the\nrefraction of a wave at the boundary between two different media.\n(HT only) Students should be able to use wave front diagrams to\nexplain refraction in terms of the change of speed that happens\nwhen a wave travels from one medium to a different medium.\nWS 1.2\nRequired practical activity 21: investigate how the amount of infrared radiation absorbed or\nradiated by a surface depends on the nature of that surface.\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 157\nAT skills covered by this practical activity: physics AT 1 and 4.\nThis practical activity also provides opportunities to develop WS and MS. Details of all skills are\ngiven in Key opportunities for skills development (page 191).\n6.6.2.3 Properties of electromagnetic waves 2\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\n(HT only) Radio waves can be produced by oscillations in electrical\ncircuits.\n(HT only) When radio waves are absorbed they may create an\nalternating current with the same frequency as the radio wave itself,\nso radio waves can themselves induce oscillations in an electrical\ncircuit.\nChanges in atoms and the nuclei of atoms can result in\nelectromagnetic waves being generated or absorbed over a wide\nfrequency range. Gamma rays originate from changes in the\nnucleus of an atom.\nUltraviolet waves, X-rays and gamma rays can have hazardous\neffects on human body tissue. The effects depend on the type of\nradiation and the size of the dose. Radiation dose (in sieverts) is a\nmeasure of the risk of harm resulting from an exposure of the body\nto the radiation.\nWS 1.5\n1000 millisieverts (mSv) = 1 sievert (Sv)\nStudents will not be required to recall the unit of radiation dose.\nStudents should be able to draw conclusions from given data about\nthe risks and consequences of exposure to radiation.\nUltraviolet waves can cause skin to age prematurely and increase\nthe risk of skin cancer. X-rays and gamma rays are ionising\nradiation that can cause the mutation of genes and cancer.\nWS 1.5\n6.6.2.4 Uses and applications of electromagnetic waves\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nElectromagnetic waves have many practical applications. For\nexample:\n• radio waves – television and radio\n• microwaves – satellite communications, cooking food\n• infrared – electrical heaters, cooking food, infrared cameras\n• visible light – fibre optic communications\n• ultraviolet – energy efficient lamps, sun tanning\n• X-rays and gamma rays – medical imaging"

Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 155\n6.6.1.2 Properties of waves\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nStudents should be able to describe wave motion in terms of their\namplitude, wavelength, frequency and period.\nThe amplitude of a wave is the maximum displacement of a point\non a wave away from its undisturbed position.\nThe wavelength of a wave is the distance from a point on one wave\nto the equivalent point on the adjacent wave.\nThe frequency of a wave is the number of waves passing a point\neach second.\nMS 1c, 3b, c\nperiod = 1\nf requency\nT = 1\nf\nperiod, T, in seconds, s\nfrequency, f, in hertz, Hz\nThe wave speed is the speed at which the energy is transferred (or\nthe wave moves) through the medium.\nAll waves obey the wave equation:\nMS 1c, 3b, c\nStudents should be able to\napply this equation which is\ngiven on the Physics\nequation sheet.\nwave speed = f requency × wavelength\nv = f λ\nwave speed, v, in metres per second, m/s\nfrequency, f, in hertz, Hz\nwavelength, λ, in metres, m\nStudents should be able to:\n• identify amplitude and wavelength from given diagrams\nMS 1c, 3b, 3c\nStudents should be able to\nrecall and apply this\nequation.\n• describe a method to measure the speed of sound waves in\nair\nAT 1\nWS 2.3, 2.4, 2.6, 2.7, 3.1,\n3.5\n• describe a method to measure the speed of ripples on a water\nsurface.\nAT 1, AT 4\nWS 2.3, 2.4, 2.6, 2.7, 3.1,\n3.5\nRequired practical activity 20: make observations to identify the suitability of apparatus to\nmeasure the frequency, wavelength and speed of waves in a ripple tank and waves in a solid and\ntake appropriate measurements.\nAT skills covered by this practical activity: physics AT 4.\n156 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\nThis practical activity also provides opportunities to develop WS and MS. Details of all skills are\ngiven in Key opportunities for skills development (page 191).\n6.6.2 Electromagnetic waves\n6.6.2.1 Types of electromagnetic waves\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nElectromagnetic waves are transverse waves that transfer energy\nfrom the source of the waves to an absorber.\nElectromagnetic waves form a continuous spectrum and all types of\nelectromagnetic wave travel at the same velocity through a vacuum\n(space) or air.\nThe waves that form the electromagnetic spectrum are grouped in\nterms of their wavelength and their frequency. Going from long to\nshort wavelength (or from low to high frequency) the groups are:\nradio, microwave, infrared, visible light (red to violet), ultraviolet, X-\nrays and gamma rays.\nOur eyes only detect visible light and so detect a limited range of\nelectromagnetic waves.\nStudents should be able to give examples that illustrate the transfer\nof energy by electromagnetic waves.\n6.6.2.2 Properties of electromagnetic waves 1\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\n(HT only) Different substances may absorb, transmit, refract or\nreflect electromagnetic waves in ways that vary with wavelength.\n(HT only) Some effects, for example refraction, are due to the\ndifference in velocity of the waves in different substances.\nStudents should be able to construct ray diagrams to illustrate the\nrefraction of a wave at the boundary between two different media.\n(HT only) Students should be able to use wave front diagrams to\nexplain refraction in terms of the change of speed that happens\nwhen a wave travels from one medium to a different medium.\nWS 1.2\nRequired practical activity 21: investigate how the amount of infrared radiation absorbed or\nradiated by a surface depends on the nature of that surface.\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 157\nAT skills covered by this practical activity: physics AT 1 and 4.\nThis practical activity also provides opportunities to develop WS and MS. Details of all skills are\ngiven in Key opportunities for skills development (page 191).\n6.6.2.3 Properties of electromagnetic waves 2\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\n(HT only) Radio waves can be produced by oscillations in electrical\ncircuits.\n(HT only) When radio waves are absorbed they may create an\nalternating current with the same frequency as the radio wave itself,\nso radio waves can themselves induce oscillations in an electrical\ncircuit.\nChanges in atoms and the nuclei of atoms can result in\nelectromagnetic waves being generated or absorbed over a wide\nfrequency range. Gamma rays originate from changes in the\nnucleus of an atom.\nUltraviolet waves, X-rays and gamma rays can have hazardous\neffects on human body tissue. The effects depend on the type of\nradiation and the size of the dose. Radiation dose (in sieverts) is a\nmeasure of the risk of harm resulting from an exposure of the body\nto the radiation.\nWS 1.5\n1000 millisieverts (mSv) = 1 sievert (Sv)\nStudents will not be required to recall the unit of radiation dose.\nStudents should be able to draw conclusions from given data about\nthe risks and consequences of exposure to radiation.\nUltraviolet waves can cause skin to age prematurely and increase\nthe risk of skin cancer. X-rays and gamma rays are ionising\nradiation that can cause the mutation of genes and cancer.\nWS 1.5\n6.6.2.4 Uses and applications of electromagnetic waves\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nElectromagnetic waves have many practical applications. For\nexample:\n• radio waves – television and radio\n• microwaves – satellite communications, cooking food\n• infrared – electrical heaters, cooking food, infrared cameras\n• visible light – fibre optic communications\n• ultraviolet – energy efficient lamps, sun tanning\n• X-rays and gamma rays – medical imaging and treatments.\n158 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration"

          "locator": "Official PDF pages 159-162; 6.7 Magnetism and electromagnetism",
          "text": "Official PDF pages 159-162\nMagnetism and electromagnetism\nElectromagnetic effects are used in a wide variety of devices. Engineers make use of the fact that\na magnet moving in a coil can produce electric current and also that when current flows around a\nmagnet it can produce movement. It means that systems that involve control or communications\ncan take full advantage of this.\n6.7.1 Permanent and induced magnetism, magnetic forces and fields\n6.7.1.1 Poles of a magnet\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThe poles of a magnet are the places where the magnetic forces\nare strongest. When two magnets are brought close together they\nexert a force on each other. Two like poles repel each other. Two\nunlike poles attract each other. Attraction and repulsion between\ntwo magnetic poles are examples of non-contact force.\nA permanent magnet produces its own magnetic field. An induced\nmagnet is a material that becomes a magnet when it is placed in a\nmagnetic field. Induced magnetism always causes a force of\nattraction. When removed from the magnetic field an induced\nmagnet loses most/all of its magnetism quickly.\nStudents should be able to describe:\n• the attraction and repulsion between unlike and like poles for\npermanent magnets\n• the difference between permanent and induced magnets.\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 159\n6.7.1.2 Magnetic fields\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThe region around a magnet where a force acts on another magnet\nor on a magnetic material (iron, steel, cobalt and nickel) is called\nthe magnetic field.\nThe force between a magnet and a magnetic material is always one\nof attraction.\nThe strength of the magnetic field depends on the distance from the\nmagnet. The field is strongest at the poles of the magnet.\nThe direction of the magnetic field at any point is given by the\ndirection of the force that would act on another north pole placed at\nthat point. The direction of a magnetic field line is from the north\n(seeking) pole of a magnet to the south(seeking) pole of the\nmagnet.\nA magnetic compass contains a small bar magnet. The Earth has a\nmagnetic field. The compass needle points in the direction of the\nEarth’s magnetic field.\nStudents should be able to:\n• describe how to plot the magnetic field pattern of a magnet\nusing a compass\n• draw the magnetic field pattern of a bar magnet showing how\nstrength and direction change from one point to another\n• explain how the behaviour of a magnetic compass is related\nto evidence that the core of the Earth must be magnetic.\nWS 2.2\n6.7.2 The motor effect\n160 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\n6.7.2.1 Electromagnetism\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nWhen a current flows through a conducting wire a magnetic field is\nproduced around the wire. The strength of the magnetic field\ndepends on the current through the wire and the distance from the\nwire.\nShaping a wire to form a solenoid increases the strength of the\nmagnetic field created by a current through the wire. The magnetic\nfield inside a solenoid is strong and uniform.\nThe magnetic field around a solenoid has a similar shape to that of\na bar magnet. Adding an iron core increases the strength of the\nmagnetic field of a solenoid. An electromagnet is a solenoid with an\niron core.\nStudents should be able to:\n• describe how the magnetic effect of a current can be\ndemonstrated\n• draw the magnetic field pattern for a straight wire carrying a\ncurrent and for a solenoid (showing the direction of the field)\n• explain how a solenoid arrangement can increase the\nmagnetic effect of the current.\nWS 2.2\n6.7.2.2 Fleming's left-hand rule (HT only)\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nWhen a conductor carrying a current is placed in a magnetic field\nthe magnet producing the field and the conductor exert a force on\neach other. This is called the motor effect.\nStudents should be able to show that Fleming's left-hand rule\nrepresents the relative orientation of the force, the current in the\nconductor and the magnetic field.\nStudents should be able to recall the factors that affect the size of\nthe force on the conductor.\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 161\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nFor a conductor at right angles to a magnetic field and carrying a\ncurrent:\nf orce = magnetic f lux density × current × length\nF = B I l\nforce, F, in newtons, N\nmagnetic flux density, B, in tesla, T\ncurrent, I, in amperes, A (amp is acceptable for ampere)\nlength, l, in metres, m\nMS 3b, c\nStudents should be able to\napply this equation which is\ngiven on the physics\nequation sheet.\n6.7.2.3 Electric motors (HT only)\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nA coil of wire carrying a current in a magnetic field tends to rotate.\nThis is the basis of an electric motor.\nStudents should be able to explain how the force on a conductor in\na magnetic field causes the rotation of the coil in an electric motor.\n6.8 Key ideas\nThe complex and diverse phenomena of the natural and man-made world can be described in\nterms of a small number of key ideas in physics.\nThese key ideas are of universal application, and we have embedded them throughout the subject\ncontent. They underpin many aspects of the science assessment and will therefore be assessed\nacross all papers.\nKey ideas in physics:\n• the use of models, as in the particle model of matter or the wave models of light and of sound\n• the concept of cause and effect in explaining such links as those between force and\nacceleration, or between changes in atomic nuclei and radioactive emissions\n• the phenomena of ‘action at a distance’ and the related concept of the field as the key to\nanalysing electrical, magnetic and gravitational effects\n• that differences, for example between pressures or temperatures or electrical potentials, are\nthe drivers of change\n• that proportionality, for example between weight and mass of an object or between force and\nextension in a spring, is an important aspect of many models in science\n• that physical laws and models are expressed in mathematical form.\n162 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration"

They underpin many aspects of the science assessment and will therefore be assessed\nacross all papers.\nKey ideas in physics:\n• the use of models, as in the particle model of matter or the wave models of light and of sound\n• the concept of cause and effect in explaining such links as those between force and\nacceleration, or between changes in atomic nuclei and radioactive emissions\n• the phenomena of ‘action at a distance’ and the related concept of the field as the key to\nanalysing electrical, magnetic and gravitational effects\n• that differences, for example between pressures or temperatures or electrical potentials, are\nthe drivers of change\n• that proportionality, for example between weight and mass of an object or between force and\nextension in a spring, is an important aspect of many models in science\n• that physical laws and models are expressed in mathematical form.\n162 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration"

          "title": "Poles of a magnet",
          "kind": "topic",
          "tier": [
            "Foundation",
            "Higher"
          ],
          "locator": "Official PDF pages 159-162; 6.7.1.1 Poles of a magnet",
          "text": "Official PDF pages 159-162\nPoles of a magnet\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThe poles of a magnet are the places where the magnetic forces\nare strongest. When two magnets are brought close together they\nexert a force on each other. Two like poles repel each other. Two\nunlike poles attract each other. Attraction and repulsion between\ntwo magnetic poles are examples of non-contact force.\nA permanent magnet produces its own magnetic field. An induced\nmagnet is a material that becomes a magnet when it is placed in a\nmagnetic field. Induced magnetism always causes a force of\nattraction. When removed from the magnetic field an induced\nmagnet loses most/all of its magnetism quickly.\nStudents should be able to describe:\n• the attraction and repulsion between unlike and like poles for\npermanent magnets\n• the difference between permanent and induced magnets.\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 159\n6.7.1.2 Magnetic fields\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nThe region around a magnet where a force acts on another magnet\nor on a magnetic material (iron, steel, cobalt and nickel) is called\nthe magnetic field.\nThe force between a magnet and a magnetic material is always one\nof attraction.\nThe strength of the magnetic field depends on the distance from the\nmagnet. The field is strongest at the poles of the magnet.\nThe direction of the magnetic field at any point is given by the\ndirection of the force that would act on another north pole placed at\nthat point. The direction of a magnetic field line is from the north\n(seeking) pole of a magnet to the south(seeking) pole of the\nmagnet.\nA magnetic compass contains a small bar magnet. The Earth has a\nmagnetic field. The compass needle points in the direction of the\nEarth’s magnetic field.\nStudents should be able to:\n• describe how to plot the magnetic field pattern of a magnet\nusing a compass\n• draw the magnetic field pattern of a bar magnet showing how\nstrength and direction change from one point to another\n• explain how the behaviour of a magnetic compass is related\nto evidence that the core of the Earth must be magnetic.\nWS 2.2\n6.7.2 The motor effect\n160 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration\n6.7.2.1 Electromagnetism\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nWhen a current flows through a conducting wire a magnetic field is\nproduced around the wire. The strength of the magnetic field\ndepends on the current through the wire and the distance from the\nwire.\nShaping a wire to form a solenoid increases the strength of the\nmagnetic field created by a current through the wire. The magnetic\nfield inside a solenoid is strong and uniform.\nThe magnetic field around a solenoid has a similar shape to that of\na bar magnet. Adding an iron core increases the strength of the\nmagnetic field of a solenoid. An electromagnet is a solenoid with an\niron core.\nStudents should be able to:\n• describe how the magnetic effect of a current can be\ndemonstrated\n• draw the magnetic field pattern for a straight wire carrying a\ncurrent and for a solenoid (showing the direction of the field)\n• explain how a solenoid arrangement can increase the\nmagnetic effect of the current.\nWS 2.2\n6.7.2.2 Fleming's left-hand rule (HT only)\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nWhen a conductor carrying a current is placed in a magnetic field\nthe magnet producing the field and the conductor exert a force on\neach other. This is called the motor effect.\nStudents should be able to show that Fleming's left-hand rule\nrepresents the relative orientation of the force, the current in the\nconductor and the magnetic field.\nStudents should be able to recall the factors that affect the size of\nthe force on the conductor.\nAQA GCSE Combined Science: Trilogy 8464. GCSE exams June 2018 onwards. Version 1.1 04 October 2019\nVisit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration 161\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nFor a conductor at right angles to a magnetic field and carrying a\ncurrent:\nf orce = magnetic f lux density × current × length\nF = B I l\nforce, F, in newtons, N\nmagnetic flux density, B, in tesla, T\ncurrent, I, in amperes, A (amp is acceptable for ampere)\nlength, l, in metres, m\nMS 3b, c\nStudents should be able to\napply this equation which is\ngiven on the physics\nequation sheet.\n6.7.2.3 Electric motors (HT only)\nContent Key opportunities for\nskills development\nA coil of wire carrying a current in a magnetic field tends to rotate.\nThis is the basis of an electric motor.\nStudents should be able to explain how the force on a conductor in\na magnetic field causes the rotation of the coil in an electric motor.\n6.8 Key ideas\nThe complex and diverse phenomena of the natural and man-made world can be described in\nterms of a small number of key ideas in physics.\nThese key ideas are of universal application, and we have embedded them throughout the subject\ncontent. They underpin many aspects of the science assessment and will therefore be assessed\nacross all papers.\nKey ideas in physics:\n• the use of models, as in the particle model of matter or the wave models of light and of sound\n• the concept of cause and effect in explaining such links as those between force and\nacceleration, or between changes in atomic nuclei and radioactive emissions\n• the phenomena of ‘action at a distance’ and the related concept of the field as the key to\nanalysing electrical, magnetic and gravitational effects\n• that differences, for example between pressures or temperatures or electrical potentials, are\nthe drivers of change\n• that proportionality, for example between weight and mass of an object or between force and\nextension in a spring, is an important aspect of many models in science\n• that physical laws and models are expressed in mathematical form.\n162 Visit aqa.org.uk/8464 for the most up-to-date specification, resources, support and administration"
